Abstract
The fatigue design philosophy of orthotropic steel decks in long span bridges has been investi-
gated. Due to their light weight, orthotropic decks have been vital for the record braking span
lengths achieved over the last 20 years. To push the development further, the design of the Izmit
Bay Bridge (IBB) has been studied with respect to fatigue, and alternative solutions have been
suggested and tested.
Detailed information about the IBB was provided by COWI, and results from a previous masterтАЩs
thesis on the bridge were acquired. A detailed and extensive ямБnite element (FE) model of the
bridge was developed. Three model detailing levels were used. One for the overall bridge design
with pre-tensioned cables and hangers, another modeling the deck segments with diaphragms
and simpliямБed orthotropic plates and ямБnally a detailed level with fully modeled orthotropic
plates and detailed diaphragm beams.
The simpliямБed orthotropic plates were modeled with
shell elements with general stiямАness matrices. Hereby, orthotropic properties could be developed
and assigned to the shell elements. The model was veriямБed trough a lot of testing. Both the
individual parts and the interaction between diямАerent parts were tested.
The FE-model was used in a fatigue investigation. Based on results from COWI, two details
and a speciямБc vehicle type were chosen for the investigation. The rib-to-deck connection and the
diaphragm cut-out were studied. Initially, hot-spot stresses from a lane centered vehicle were
found. The result was used to create inямВuence surfaces for the critical details. Hereby, the worst
case wheel load location could be identiямБed and the maximum hot-spot stresses were calculated.
Huge dependency on the wheel load location was found.
The extensiveness of the FE-model used for a fatigue investigation was studied by comparing
the full non-linear model with four alternatives. Taking into account the overall geometry was
found to have big inямВuence on the hot-spot stresses. Too simpliямБed boundary conditions of local
models were found to introduce additional ямВexibility/stiямАness, ultimately reducing/increasing
the calculated hot-spot stresses. Dependency on the longitudinal distance to a hanger was also
indicated.
In the search for an improved design, the sandwich plate system (SPS), consisting of steel face
plates and an elastomer core, was found promising. Inspired from the use of SPS in retroямБtting,
initially, the top deck plate was replaced by an SPS plate. Increased bending stiямАness led to
substantially reduced hot-spot stresses. However, huge shear deformations were found in the core
following a low elastic stiямАness of the elastomer. Increased core thickness reduced the diямАerential
deямВections between rib walls, but due to a high shear to bending deformation ratio, the SPS
was found to be a questionable solution.
A new stiямАer core material was justiямБed and the top deck plate was once again replaced with a
sandwich plate. Good performance was found. The hot-spot stresses were radically reduced and
V
==PAGE:8==
acceptable diямАerential deformations were seen without increasing the weight of the orthotropic
panels.
Finally, a new concept was introduced, exploiting the advantages of sandwich plates.
The
orthotropic plate and the diaphragms in the original design were replaced by sandwich plates
connected by a space truss. The performance of the sandwich plate exposed to wheel loads was
studied with a continuum shell FE-model. Simple iterative optimization was used on the truss
system to lower the weight. The performance of the ямБnal system was assessed with a тАЩtraямГc jamтАЩ
situation and compared to the original design. The weight of the new concept was found 20%
larger than the original. However, the concept is not rejected. A huge potential for optimization
was found and a considerable amount of simpliямБcation needs to be elaborated. The concept is
found to have potential to redeямБne the design philosophy for long span bridges.

DesignямБlosoямБen for udmattelse i ortotrope st├еld├жk i store broer unders├╕ges. Grundet en lav
v├жgt har ortotrope d├жk v├жret vitale for de rekords├жttende sp├жnd, som er opn├еet igennem de
sidste 20 ├еr. For at skubbe yderligere til den udvikling unders├╕ges Izmit Bay Bridge (IBB) for
udmattelse, og der foresl├еs og testes alternative l├╕sninger.
Detaljeret information om IBB blev givet af COWI, og resultater fra et tidligere speciale om
broen blev tilvejebragt. En detaljeret og vidtg├еende elementmetodemodel (FE-model) af broen
blev lavet med tre detaljeniveauer. Et for det overordnede brodesign med forsp├жndte kabler
og h├жngere, et andet til modellering af d├жksegmenter med tv├жrafstivere og simpliямБcerede or-

jerede tv├жrafstivninger. Den simpliямБcerede ortotrope plade blev modeleret med skalelementer
med generelle stivhedsmatricer. Herved kunne ortotrope egenskaber udvikles og tildeles skalele-
menterne.
Modelen blev veriямБceret med mange forskellige tests.
B├еde individuelle dele og
interaktionen mellem forskellige dele blev testet.
FE-modellen blev brugt i en udmattelsesanalyse. To detaljer og en k├╕ret├╕jstype blev udpeget til
unders├╕gelsen ud fra COWIs resultater. Trug-til-d├жk samlingen og udsk├жringen i tv├жrafstiverne
blev unders├╕gt. F├╕rst blev hot-spot sp├жndinger, grundet et k├╕ret├╕j placeret midt i k├╕rebanen,
fundet. Resultatet blev s├е brugt til at skabe inямВuensямВader for de kritiske detaljer. Herved kunne
den v├жrst mulige placering af hjultrykkene identiямБceres, og de maksimale hot-spot sp├жndinger
beregnes. Der blev fundet stor afh├жngighed af placeringen af hjultrykkene.
Hvor vidtg├еende FE-modelen b├╕r v├жre i en udmattelsesunders├╕gelse blev studeret ved at sam-
menligne en fuld ikke-line├жr model med ямБre alternativer. Inkludering af den overordnede stivhed
af broen viste sig at have stor indямВydelse p├е hot-spot sp├жndingerne. For simpliямБcerede rand-
betingelser i mindre modeller viste sig at introducere ekstra ямВexibilitet/stivhed, hvilket reduc-
erede/for├╕gede hot-spot sp├жndingerne. Det blev ogs├е indikeret, at sp├жndingen er afh├жngig af
den langsg├еende afstand til en h├жnger.
Dern├жst blev der s├╕gt efter et forbedret design. Sandwichpladesystemet (SPS) best├еende af ydre
st├еlplader og en elastomerkerne blev fundet lovende. Inspireret af brugen af SPS i retroямБtting
blev toppladen f├╕rst og fremmest fors├╕gt erstattet med en SPS-plade. Grundet en ├╕get b├╕jn-
ingsstivhed reduceredes hot-spot sp├жndingerne drastisk.
Der var dog store forskydningsde-
formationer i kernen, grundet elastomerens lave elasticitetsmodul. For├╕get tykkelse af kernen
reducerede de diямАerentielle nedb├╕jninger mellem trugkroppe. Grundet en h├╕j forskydnings- til
b├╕jningsdeformationsratio blev SPSтАЩen dog vurderet til at v├жre en tvivlsom l├╕sning.
Grundet en lav forskydningsstivhed i SPS-kernematerialet blev et nyt kernemateriale forsl├еet og
begrundet. Top-d├жk-pladen blev igen erstattet med en sandwichplade med god pr├жstation til
f├╕lge. Hot-spot sp├жndingerne blev radikalt reduceret og der blev fundet acceptable diямАerentielle
ямВytninger uden at for├╕ge v├жgten af de ortotrope paneler.

Til sidst blev et nyt koncept introduceret som udnytter fordelene ved sandwichplader. De or-
totrope plader og tv├жrafstivningerne i det oprindelige design blev udskiftet med sandwichplader
forbundet af et rumgitter. Sandwichpladernes opf├╕rsel blev vurderet under hjullast med en kon-
tinuum skalmodel. Simpel iterativ optimering blev udf├╕rt p├е gittersystemet for at opn├е en s├е
lav v├жgt som muligt. Det endelige system blev vurderet i en тАЩtraямБkpropтАЩ-situation og sammen-
lignet med det originale design. V├жgten af det nye design blev fundet til at v├жre 20% st├╕rre
end det originale. Dog afvises konceptet ikke, da der ses et stort potentiale for optimering og en
ikke ubetydelig del simpliямБcering som skal uddybes. Konceptet har potentiale til at redeямБnere
designямБlosoямБen for store broer.

1
Introduction
Since the 1950s, orthotropic steel decks have been used for long span bridges. Due to their
light weight, in comparison with concrete decks, orthotropic deck designs have been vital for the
increasing span lengths. Much research have been done and huge developments have been seen
trough the years. The Izmit Bay Bridge (see front page), a long span bridge with one of the
most optimized orthotropic steel decks, is currently under construction. Once built, the bridge
will have the 5th longest main span in the world.
Two aspects are found to lead the development in orthotropic steel decks; the stiямАness required,
to prevent cracking of the surfacing, and a desire to increase the distance between diaphragms
to reduce the number of complicated details. The ямБrst aspect sets requirements to the thickness
of the top deck plate mostly based on experience without many options for optimization. The
second aspect has been studied a lot over the years. The distance between diaphragms is 4m
in the Far├╕ Bridge and in the Great Belt Bridge, built in the 80s and 90s, respectively, while
the Izmit Bay Bridge will have a record making distance of 5m between diaphragms. Increasing
the distance between diaphragms, naturally, increases the required stiямАness of the orthotropic
plates. However, a feasible solution was found for the Izmit Bay Bridge due to the reduced
number of diaphragms with complicated detailing.
One of the greatest concerns, when using orthotropic decks, is fatigue. Considerable local de-
formation is seen in the top deck plate from wheel loads. The orthotropic decks includes a lot
of welding and critical details, such as cut-outs in the diaphragms, all prone to fatigue. Fatigue
is the limiting state at several details. It it therefore a necessity to consider fatigue in order to
develop the design further.
The biggest expense through the whole construction phase of orthotropic steel decks is the
fabrication of deck segments. Cost estimates of the orthotropic deck in the Great Belt Bridge
are shown in Appendix A. All costs are adjusted with the price development for steel structures
according to Dansk Statistik [1]. It is estimated that the total cost of the main bridge deck of
the Great Belt Bridge, today, would be 1100 million DKK. Fabrication of the orthotropic panels
is estimated to represent 17% of the total cost while assembling of the panels to deck segments
represents 27%. Meanwhile is all deliverance of steel covered by only 18% of the total cost.
This means that reducing fabrication costs would have a huge impact. The total deck segment
fabrication cost is approximately 500 million DKK. The many complicated welds are the main
reason for the huge expense.
Beside a potential for reducing fabrication costs, reducing the amount of steel used in the deck
would aямАect the whole bridge system. Dead load is the main load contributor in cables and
hangers, thus, a reduced weight of the deck would lead to smaller cables and hangers.
For
every two kilograms in the deck, one kilogram of steel is placed in the cables. Hereby, in total
approximately 45 million DKK would be saved by a 10% reduction of steel in the deck, if solely
steel deliverance is considered (Appendix A).
==PAGE:16==
2
1
Introduction
(a) Great Belt Bridge.
(b) Izmit Bay Bridge.
Figure 1.1: Inside orthotropic steel decks.
Building a long span bridge is a huge expense and in the end always paid by the public, whether
it is through taxes or road pricing. Therefore, engineers must do their absolute best to handle
and use the money the best way possible. As engineers we must be creative and visionary to
come up with new solutions.
Based on the background study in section 2 and knowledge gained during the project it is
assessed that the orthotropic deck system, as it is designed today, is near its full potential. It is
therefore of interest to be creative and look for a possible new structural system for long span
bridge decks.
The Izmit Bay Bridge is used as a reference in the search for an improved deck solution for long
span bridges. First, a detailed and extensive ямБnite element (FE) model is made. Shell elements
with orthotropic behavior are developed to represent orthotropic plates in a semi-detailed model
connecting a detailed shell model and an overall beam model.
Second, assessment methods
in fatigue are elaborated and the load application is considered. Third, the current design of
the Izmit Bay Bridge is studied to better understand the advantages and disadvantages of the
orthotropic deck system. Additionally, insight on how to model the bridge is revealed. The
investigation is followed by ideas on how to improve the design. Sandwich plates are found to be
a promising solution, and diямАerent approaches are studied. Shear deformations are found to be
signiямБcant, and requirements to the core material are deямБned. Finally, a new concept exploiting
the beneямБts of sandwich plates is suggested with a space truss. Initial studies are made on the
new concept through comparison with the original design. The potential for further development
is evaluated, and it is assessed whether the concept is realistic.
==PAGE:17==
2
Background Study
A lot of research has been conducted on orthotropic steel decks over the last 60 years. Be-
fore starting the project a huge literature study was made to identify what has already been
done. Naturally, it is less interesting to derive conclusions which could have been found in the
literature.
Fatigue cracks have been reported to occur in a large number of orthotropic decks all around
the world [2, 3, 4], and huge eямАorts have been made on how to prevent cracking.
During
the 1990s, lots of research were conducted for the Eurocodes to present fatigue categories and
develop guidelines for speciямБc details [5]. Mostly, the nominal stress approach is used for fatigue
veriямБcation [4, 6, 7], but due to the easy access to reямБned FE-models is it of interest to see
whether other methods could be suitable for the fatigue assessment.
A such method is the
hot-spot stress approach, however, it is still relatively unclear to which fatigue criterion the
calculated stresses should be compared [8]. Fracture mechanics have been suggested to be used
with detailed submodels [9]. However, due to the complexity of the orthotropic deck versus the
applicability of the analysis results, a more simpliямБed and therefore conservative approach is
inherently present [8].
Parametric investigations are often used to study the eямАect of changing thicknesses of diямАerent
members [6, 7, 10, 11, 12, 13, 14]. Due to limited ideas on how to optimize the concept, typically,
the thickness of the top deck plate and the ribs are changed a couple of millimeters in order to
see the inямВuence. This often results in simple obvious conclusions such as that a thicker deck
plate will reduce the considered stress range.
One of the most critical details in orthotropic decks is the web cut-outs in the diaphragms. The
cut-outs ensure that the ribs are continuous and are usually made with cope-holes around the
bottom of the rib, but also tight ямБtting is permitted having a weld along the cut-out around the
rib periphery [15]. Throughout this project only cut-outs with cope-holes are considered. The
stress concentrations rises on the edge of the cut-out as seen in Figure 2.1. The magnitude of
the stress is highly dependent on the shape of the cut-out, which have been under investigation
several times over the past 25 years [16].
Connor [10] examines the shape of the cut-out and suggests a more тАЩliberalтАЩ cut-out geometry
opposed to current AASHTO recommendations [18] when using bulkheads.
An alternative
approach of investigating cut-outs is presented by Corte & Bogaert [19] excluding bulkheads.
Hereby a Vierendel truss approach can be used, where shape factors are calculated for diямАerent
cut-outs by use of ямБnite elements. Several cut-out shapes are investigated parametrically by
Corte [20] using the Vierendel truss approach. It is found that the stresses decreases with both
rib height as well as with the rib inclination, and that the optimum cut-out geometry is highly
dependent on the type of loading.
Another way of improving the performance near the cut-outs is by using bulkheads inside the
ribs at the diaphragms. Fatigue tests using curved bulkheads are reported by Oh et al. [11]
==PAGE:18==
4
2
Background Study
Figure 2.1: Typical tangential stresses at the periphery of the ямВoor-beam cut-out for a trapezoidal rib
without an internal diaphragm in the plane of the ямВoor-beam web. Stresses shown are due to a 200-kN
load placed on the ямВoor beam between the ribs (ref.: [17]).
and Oh & Bae [12] where stress concentration are reduced signiямБcantly. Bulkheads, as well
as internal stiямАeners, have also been used for retroямБtting to reduce the critical stresses [21,
22].
From experiences based on the full scale fatigue tests of the Williamsburg Bridge and
the Bronx-Whitestone Bridge, Connor & Fisher [23] examines how to deямБne and determine
fatigue stress ranges at the rib-to-diaphragm connection when including bulkheads. However,
typically bulkheads are not used on new bridges as potential misalignment between bulkheads
and diaphragm beam webs could create critical situations [24]. Also, it would be hard to reach
the bulkhead connections for inspection and reparation once the deck is installed. Therefore,
in general, bulkheads are an option solely used when the diaphragm height is limited e.g. in
retroямБtting as it was the case with the Williamburg Bridge in New York City [15].
Beside improving all the detailing, it is important to look for options which reduce the amount
of detailing. Not only this would introduce less critical details but more importantly, a great
amount of money can be saved. Especially, the many welds are a huge expense which should be
considered in order to improve the design [24, 25, 26].
==PAGE:19==
3
Izmit Bay Bridge
The Izmit Bay Bridge (IBB) is used as a basis for the investigation. IBB is currently under
construction in Turkey and is expected to be completed in 2017. The bridge is located approxi-
mately 50km from Istanbul at the eastern end of the Sea of Marmara. The bridge is part of the
Gebze-Izmir highway project. IBB is designed by Danish engineering design ямБrm COWI A/S
and architecture ямБrm Dissing+Weitling.
The bridge is a suspension bridge with a 1550m long main span and 566m long side spans. The
pylon height is approx. 240m. A side-view of the bridge is seen in Figure 3.1 and 3.2. The
geometry shown refer to the ямБnal state of the bridge, thus, when dead load is acting. To achieve
the geometry, the cables and hangers needs to be pre-tensioned.
The proper pre-tensioning
is found through an iterative process, which can be most challenging. The pre-tensioning is
included in the investigation, but to limit the scope of the thesis, results are taken from a
previous masterтАЩs thesis on the exact same bridge [27].
The girder and pylons are made of steel while the foundations and anchor blocks are made
of concrete. The foundations and anchor blocks are considered as supports and not discretely
modeled in the FE-model.
Figure 3.1: Izmit Bay Bridge.

The IBB will carry 6 lanes of motorway. A typical cross section with division of the lanes is seen
in Figure 3.3. The outer lane containing heavier traямГc is investigated throughout the report.
The thickness of the surfacing is 60mm. The girder is a box girder made of 25m long segments,
which are assembled mid-way between hangers. The geometry of the girder varies along the
bridge. The typical deck section in between pylons is 35.9m wide including inspection walkways.
The maximum height at the line of symmetry is 4.75m. Diaphragms are installed for every 5m.
The diaphragms transfer the load out to the hangers and ensure a high torsional stiямАness of
the deck section. Internal inclined rods are installed connecting the upper and lower diaphragm
beam. The longitudinal stiямАness between diaphragms is ensured by orthotropic plates made with
ribs spaced 600mm on center. Detailed cross sections of a typical deck section and a diaphragm
are shown in Figure 3.4. The geometry of the rib type used under the heaviest loaded traямГc
lane is shown in Figure 3.5, while the geometry of the cut-out in the diaphragm is shown in
Figure 3.6.
The pylons consist of two slightly inclined pylon legs connected by an upper and a lower cross
beam. The two pylons are identical with geometry as seen in Figure 3.7. The bridge girder is
installed so that only transverse support is ensured at the pylons.

4
FEM Modeling
The basis for the project is a ямБnite element model made in Abaqus [28]. For the model to produce
usable results many considerations have to be made. In a fatigue calculation local stresses are
of most importance. Thus, the orthotropic plates and diaphragm beams have to be modeled in
detail at the locations of interest. Naturally, the mesh of the model at these locations needs to
be reямБned to give converged results.
Previous projects have indicated that vehicular loads, which introduce fatigue, have a very local
impact on the orthotropic deck. The importance of a reямБned model at a distance to the location
of interest is therefore limited. For that reason many research projects in orthotropic decks limit
the model to a few couple of sections.
The ямБrst problem to consider is how to introduce boundary conditions. Some projects assume
that the diaphragm beams/cross beams are supported vertically at each end as seen in Figure 4.1.
By doing this, too stiямАsupports are introduced, resulting in higher local stresses [29]. Therefore,
it is of interest to develop a model which models the boundary of the detailed orthotropic plate
with the diaphragm beams in an appropriate way.

4.1
Model Description
As the stiямАness of the bridge deck depends on the whole system, it is of interest to model the
entire bridge to see the inямВuence of the boundary conditions. Naturally, a detailed model of the
entire bridge would be too computational expensive. Instead, a model based on three diямАerent
levels of detailing was developed.
As mentioned, a fully detailed model of the orthotropic plate with diaphragm beams is required
to asses the local fatigue stresses. This part of the model is called the level 3 model and includes
all geometries of the design, but excludes small details such as welds. As the area of interest
is rather small, it is of less importance how detailed the model is a short distance from it.
Therefore, the level 3 model is about as big as suggested in previous research [9, 12, 29].
The level 3 model is connected to a model consisting of shells and beams. This part of the
model is called the level 2 model. The shells approximate the orthotropic plates while the beams
approximate the diaphragm beams. In order to represent the orthotropic plates and diaphragm
beams with the shell and beam elements, their properties are studied.
The level 2 model extents a couple of segments, but to model a 2.7km long bridge a third level
is needed. The deck segments modeled with shells and beams connect to a beam model, where
the girder, pylons, main cables and hangers are made with beam elements. This model is called
the level 1 model.
Level 1 model
The basic level of the model is a beam model, which includes the entire bridge with pylons,
cables, hangers and the part of the deck not included in the level 2 and 3 model. The level 1
model is seen in Figure 4.2.
To obtain the correct stiямАness, the cables and hangers are pre-tensioned and self weight is
introduced. A non-linear calculation is required. If pre-tensioning was not to be used the bridge
would deямВect several meters from dead load alone. It is a rather complicated procedure to ямБnd
the pre-tensioning of every single cable-part and hanger and not within the scope of this project.
Therefore, it was decided to use the result from another project made on the same bridge.
Andersen & Hansen [27] analyzed the exact same bridge. The ямБrst part of the project was to
calculate the pre-tensioning in each cable, and a Matlab routine was made for this purpose. To
simplify the problem, only half the bridge was modeled as a 2D-model. This was done by using
only one pylon-leg and halving all properties of the deck. The main cable was divided into several
smaller linear parts connecting each end of the hangers all being prescribed with a pre-tensioning.
Each hanger also had an individual pre-tensioning, resulting in 204 pre-tensioning stresses for
half of the bridge. Through an iterative process it was ensured that the deck remained in its

intended position. Pre-tensions were applied in Abaqus as bolt-loads by adjusting the length of

and each hanger can be seen in Appendix D. The deformed bridge and the deformed deck alone

pre-tensions found for the IBB2012-model are used. The density of each element, which causes
the dead load, leading to the pre-tensioning, needs to be consistent with what was used in the
IBB2012-model. For the towers, main cables and hangers, simply the steel density is used. For
the deck, surfacing needs to be taking into account. This has been done consistently with the
IBB2012-model in Appendix E.
Level 2 model
The primary objective for the level 2 model is to model the boundaries for the level 3 model.
Thus, the plates and diaphragm beams connecting to the level 3 model are the most important.
The level 2 model is seen in Figure 4.4.
The level 2 model consists of shell and beam elements. The shells model the orthotropic plates.
To do so as correct as possible the general stiямАness matrix section is used. The general stiямАness
matrix is symmetric, thus, big assumptions must be made to simplify the problem. In section

Two types of beams are used, one for the diaphragm beams, which are rather complicated due
to the spikes, and one for the internal rods, which are simple circular hollow sections. The
internal rods are modeled using a beam section with a pipe proямБle while the diaphragm beams
are modeled with generalized proямБles.
By using a generalized proямБle, all properties can be
prescribed individually. The properties are found in section 4.3.
To ensure correct behavior, tests of the coupling between the shells and beams are made in section
4.5.1. In section 4.5.2 the properties of the of the total deck is compared to the properties found
by COWI.
The self weight of the level 2 model must be consistent with the IBB2012-model to ensure the
right behavior from the pre-tensioning. The dead loads found for the level 1 model are distributed
on the shells of the level 2 model. This is done in Appendix E. It is noted that distributing the
self weight solely to the orthotropic plates is assumed suямГcient. Thus, no densities are deямБned
for the diaphragm beams.
Level 3 model
The third level is modeled with all details from the geometry given by COWI. The level 3 model
is shown in Figure 4.5. The homogeneous shell section is used with prescribed shell thicknesses.
Only a small part of the model is of interest, thus, the mesh varies to limit the number of
elements. A discussion on the issue of meshing can be found in section 4.6.
To simplify the model it is assumed suямГcient to apply the dead load to the deck plate only. The
density consistent with the IBB2012-model is calculated in Appendix E.
Connections
It is important to carefully consider the connections between elements within the same model as
well as the connections between the three models. A walk-through of all the diямАerent kinds of
connections is given in section 4.4. Initially, before making the bridge model, simple tests were
made on some of the connections to ensure that the connections were possible to make. These
tests are shown in Appendix I. Further, the connections are considered and tested in section
4.5.

4.2
Orthotropic Plate

shell stiямАness section. A symmetric 6 by 6 element stiямАness matrix, D, needs to be deямБned. The
constitutive relations used in Abaqus are written in matrix form in Eq. (1).

Analytical simpliямБed solutions exist, considering the plate as an isotropic plate with some extra
stiямАness added in the strong-axis direction from the ribs. As the elastic center will not be the

results. As the plate is highly orthotropic, coupling terms between axial stiямАness and bending
stiямАness will occur.

4.2.1
Analytical solutions
Analytic solutions of the shell stiямАnesses used for the Abaqus model have been found based
on the theory of orthotropic plates presented in Nielsen [30].
The only deviation is for the
twist stiямАness which for orthotropic plates is presented separately for each direction. As the
stiямАness matrix seen in Eq. (1) uses the relation mxy = myx = Dxy2╬║xy (ref. [28]) a combined
approximate twist stiямАness must be found. It was found that an average of the stiямАness in the
two directions gave a usable solution.
It is noted that the the ямБllets of the corners in the ribs are ignored in the analytical calcula-
tions. Hereby, a bit larger axial stiямАness and bending stiямАness in the strong axis direction are
expected.
As no coupling between axial stiямАness and bending stiямАness exists analytically, the form of the
stiямАness matrix is as seen in Eq. (3).

4.2.2
Test plate model
The Abaqus test model consist of 8 ribs, whereby the orthotropic plate is 4.8m wide. The length
of the plate is 4.8m as well, as a squared plate is preferable.
To ensure simply supports without locking any motions, 6 degree of freedoms (DOFs) must be
constrained. 5 translational and 1 rotational DOFs were chosen for stretching and bending,
while 6 translational DOFs were chosen for shearing and twisting. The supports can be seen in
the set-up ямБgures in the following sections. The meshing has been investigated for all tests to
ensure converged results, examples thereof are presented in Appendix G. It was found that the
required mesh size was strongly dependent on the type of test.
Stretching and bending coupling terms are expected, as it is necessary to deямБne a single reference
plane to which the stiямАnesses are referred. To ease the further modeling, the center of the top
plate is chosen. In the analytical solution in Appendix F, the elastic center was found to be
placed 100mm below the center of the top plate. Therefore, a quite big coupling term between
strong-axis stretching and strong-axis bending is expected.
The loads are applied to reference points which are connected to the ends of the orthotropic
plate by use of coupling constraints. The reference points are located in the reference plane. For
each case solely the relevant DOFs are constrained. Hereby, the plate is able to give information
about the coupling terms, as the plate is able to deform freely.
Measurement points are deямБned to measure the deformations. As the plate is orthotropic, local
distortion in the deck plate can be expected between rib webs. Only the twisting test shows
noticeable distortion, thus, an average of several point is chosen for this test. To evade distortion
from boundary conditions at the edge of the plate, all measurements are made at least a rib-
section distance, 0.6m, from the edge. For all tests, expect for twisting, the edge midpoints
==PAGE:32==
18
4
FEM Modeling
a rib-section distance inside the plate are used as measurement points. For twisting all nodes
located more than a rib-section distance from the edge are used for the averaging.
It is noted that the Abaqus model was made so that the strong-axis direction is in the y-axis
while the weak-axis is in the x-axis. As this is opposite to the rest of the report this is only seen
in the Figures. The results are presented in the same manner as the rest of the report.
4.2.3
Stretching
Firstly, the terms related to axial deformation are found by use of the Abaqus test plate model.
Axial forces are applied at the reference points at each end of the plate. It is noted that the
reference points are located in the plane of the top deck plate. The reference points are used to
distribute the normal force to the entire plate end section. The coupling between the reference
point and the plate is only made on the relevant translational DOF. The test set-up of the the
stretching test in the strong-axis and weak-axis direction can be seen in Figure 4.7. In both
cases a force of 106N is applied at each end. It is noted that the forces are in equilibrium. A
look on the reactions of the deformed model conямБrms this. The deformed plate is seen in Figure
4.8.
The deformations are measured at the measurement points described in the previous section.

4.2.4
Shearing
The shearing term, C33, is found by applying in plane edge loads on all four sides of the plate
as seen in Figure 4.9. The magnitude of the four loads is 106N. The loads are applied through
the reference points, ensuring a distributed application of the load to the entire edge of the
plate. Again, force equilibrium is ensured thus, no reactions are found in the system. As the
plate is orthotropic, a twist occurs if the force is not located at the right out-of-plane position.
This is a sort of shear center. To ямБnd this location an iterative procedure was used. A location
22mm below the center of the deck plate on all four sides was found to give minimal twist. The
deformed plate is seen in Figure 4.10.
From Eq. (2) it seen that only the shear angle is needed to determine C33. The shear angle is
found from the in-plane displacements seen in Figure 4.10. By using the displacements at the
measurement points, the two angles giving the shear angle are found to be

4.2.8
VeriямБcation
Before using the simple orthotropic plate element developed in the preceding sections, several
tests are made to verify the behavior of the element. The 6 by 6 general stiямАness matrix presented
in Eq. 6 is used as the section of a shell element. In the following, two tests are made of the
element. Further assessment is made when the interaction with the diaphragm beams is studied
in section 4.5.1.
Strong-axial load
Initially, it is tested whether the simpliямБed orthotropic plate model is able to produce cor-
rect results when strong-axial load is placed in the analytically calculated elastic center. Hereby
minimal strong-axis bending is expected and the coupling term between stretching and bending
can be tested.
To ensure free deformations only 6 DOFs were constrained, 5 translational and 1 rotational,
similar to the set-up of the stretching tests in section 4.2.3. The plate is loaded with a force
of 106N in the analytically calculated strong-axis elastic center. For the simple test plate this
corresponds to a normal force of 106N and a moment of magnitude тИТ9.30├Ч104Nm as the elastic

Good agreement is found for the strains. The diямАerences are less than 0.1%. The main objective

However, the coupling curvatures do not give as good results. A huge diямАerence of 29% is found
on the the weak-axis curvature. This is a result of the need of having a symmetric stiямАness
matrix. The two coupling terms in the stiямАness matrix (D15 and D51) diямАers by 50% before
averaging, thus inaccurate results will follow as seen from this test. The diямАerence seen on the
strong-axis curvature is likely to be of the same reason. As expected no perfect results comes
from simplifying a complicated problem, but as the main objective of the this test was reached
by getting a good stretching behavior under axial load, the errors seen on the secondary coupling
eямАects are accepted.
Uniform load
The second test is to apply uniform load on the plate, and compare the deямВections found by the
two solutions. Six diямАerent boundary conditions (BC) have been tested; clamped and simply
supported on all four sides (CCCC and SSSS, respectively), clamped and simply support at the
two ends giving strong-axis bending (CFCF and SFSF, respectively) and clamped and simply
support at the two ends giving weak-axis bending (FCFC and FSFS, respectively). The uniform
load is applied on the deck plate in the full model. The load is of magnitude 106N/m2. It is
noted that for weak-axis bending the magnitude of the load results in huge deямВections. A linear
calculation was used, thus, for the purpose of verifying the simple model the deямВections were
accepted. The results of the test can be seen in Figure 4.18 and Table 4.3.

It is seen that the simple plate model is stiямАer than the full model as smaller deямВections are
found from the uniform load test. From Figure 4.18a and 4.18b showing the four-sided supported
plates, a similar behavior is seen between the full and the simple model. Local deformations are
seen in between the rib webs of the full model, thus, the minimum deямВection used for comparison
in Table 4.3 is at the rib webs.
The maximum deямВection is in the middle of the plate. A diямАerence of 15% is seen for the CCCC-
plate while a smaller diямАerence of 6% is seen for the SSSS-plate. Similar results are found for
the plate with two supports giving strong-axis bending. For the clamped plates a diямАerence of
15% is big and a reason for the diямАerence must be found. For weak-axis bending seen in Figure
4.18e and 4.18f much better results are found with diямАerences less than 0.5%.
To ямБnd diямАerences between the full model and the simple model, the deямВection along the center
of the plate in the strong-axis direction is compared for the CCCC-plate in Figure 4.19. The
deямВection of the simple plate has been multiplied by a factor of 1.15, whereby it is seen that the
the main diямАerence in deямВection is found near the support. By looking at the shear stains in the
full model, the major reason for the diямАerence is found. The shear strains are shown in Figure
4.20. The larger deямВection in the full model is due to shear deformations, which up to this point
have not been investigated. None of the stiямАnesses found for the 6 by 6 general stiямАness matrix
contains information about shear deformations. The thickness to length ratio is about 1:13.
In general it is said that the ratio must be in order of 1:100 before shear deformations can be
neglected. Also it is seen that no problems appear for bending in the weak-axis direction as the
height of the plate in this direction is determined by the deck plate thickness, thus the thickness
to length ratio is more than 1:300.
As a simple test an extra long orthotropic plate of 25m was made and tested as the ones presented
in Figure 4.18c and 4.18d. The height of the rib to length ratio was 1:69 whereby the inямВuence
of shear deformations should be limited. The results was clear as the diямАerences between the
full model and the simple model of the clamped plate were as low as 1.1%. It became clear
that shear deformations had to be taken into account, thus, in the following section, the shear
stiямАnesses are calculated and a new comparison is made.

4.2.9
Including transverse shear stiямАness
Using the general shell stiямАness gives an opportunity to deямБne the transverse shear stiямАnesses,
K11, K12 and K22. To proceed with the model as fast as possible it was chosen only to consider
the transverse shear stiямАnesses analytically. The general constitutive relation for transverse shear
is seen in Eq. 7.
qii = Kij╬│jj
i = 1..2,
j = 1..2
(7)
As the ribs are closed an estimate of the strong-axis transverse shear stiямАness can be based
on the rib webs alone, as the horizontal members will have a minimal inямВuence. Hereby the
strong-axis transverse shear stiямАness of TP1 is found as follows.
K11 = 2hrtr
dr
G = 2 ┬╖ 0.360m ┬╖ 0.007m
0.600m
80.8GPa = 6.79 ├Ч 108NmтИТ1
The weak-axis transverse shear stiямАness can be determined based on the deck plate alone as
follows.
K22 = khG = 5
6 ┬╖ 0.014m ┬╖ 80.8GPa = 9.42 ┬╖ 108NmтИТ1
According to Nielsen [30] the coupling stiямАness can be neglected, thus K12 = 0.
After having implemented the transverse shear stiямАness in the general shell stiямАness section, the
uniform load tests from section 4.2.8 can be repeated. The results of the tests are shown in
Figure 4.21 and Table 4.4.
By using the analytically estimated transverse shear stiямАness, the results have improved. Espe-
cially the clamped plates were inямВuenced by the shear deformations and by simply including the
analytically calculated stiямАnesses the diямАerences are below 2%. Considering the huge simpliямБca-
tion of the problem, the results are acceptable and no further tests are made on the transverse
shear stiямАness. If the last 2% was to be found, a test model exposed to transverse shear could be
developed so that the transverse shear stiямАness could be found from the full model. Hereby, the
small contribution from the deck plate and the bottom plate of the rib could be included.
4.2.10
Comparison between the Abaqus model and the analytical model
The deck of the Izmit Bay Bridge consists of several orthotropic plates with diямАerent dimensions.
In order not to make all the tests presented above for all the diямАerent plates, it is of interest
to see whether analytical solutions can be used as approximations for the stiямАness parameters.
Therefore, the results from the Abaqus test model are compared to the analytical results found
in section 4.2.1.
Naturally, the two models are very diямАerent as the test based model includes the coupling terms
and uses a single reference plane. The analytical model simply ignores that the location of the
neutral line is diямАerent for the two directions. Obviously, this would lead to diямАerent results in
the ямБnal model. In Table 4.5 a comparison between the analytically calculated stiямАnesses and
the stiямАnesses found through testing is presented. The coupling terms of the test model are
ignored, whereby the shear and the twist stiямАness are the only properties that can be compared
directly as no relating coupling terms exist. The relative diямАerences are with respect to the
Abaqus model.

Firstly, the shear and twist stiямАnesses are evaluated.
The shear stiямАness found by the test
model is 8% larger than the one calculated analytically. This is due to the ribs being ignored
in the analytical solution. The twist stiямАness is a bit bigger when calculated analytically but
with a diямАerence less than 2%, the twist seems to be the best performing property.
Much
uncertainty was related to the analytically calculated twist stiямАness, as no general rules exist
on how to describe an orthotropic plate with a 6 by 6 general stiямАness matrix. According to
Nielsen [30] the twist should be uncoupled so that two constitutive relations exist, one for each
direction. Hereby, the transverse twist stiямАness is calculated based on the deck plate only, while
the longitudinal twist stiямАness includes the ribs. In order to have an approximate analytical
solution, simply an average of the two stiямАnesses was used. As seen from the result in Table
4.5 this turns out to be an acceptable solution. The reason, for the analytically calculated twist
stiямАness being too high, can relate to the fact that the geometry used for the analytical solution
ignores the ямБllets of the ribs. Hereby a too big enclosed area is used when calculating the rib
twist stiямАness parameter (see Appendix F).
For the stretching and bending terms greater stiямАnesses were found from the tests than the ones
calculated analytically. This relates to the coupling stiямАnesses. In order to take into account
the impact of the coupling stiямАnesses, it is relevant to compare deformations under the basic
deformation modes. Therefore, a small shell model is made with a general shell stiямАness section
with the analytical properties. The test set-ups are similar to the set-ups for the stretching and
bending tests in section 4.2.3 and 4.2.5, respectively. The results are shown in Table 4.6.
Smaller relative diямАerences are found on all main deformations in each deformation mode, mean-
ing ╬╡11 under strong-axis stretching, ╬╡22 under weak-axis stretching, etc. This is due to the inямВu-
ence of the coupling stiямАnesses. The diямАerence is especially a lot better for strong-axis bending,
where the strong-axis curvature in the analytical solution is 5% smaller than the curvature found
by the Abaqus model. The analytical solution is stiямАer than the Abaqus model in bending. This
complies with the exclusion of the ямБllets in the ribs in the analytical model, whereby a stiямАer
model is excepted.

The weak-axis axial stiямАness is smaller when calculated analytically. This is due to ribs being

With relative diямАerences up to 10% it can be argued that general stiямАness

level 2 model, in which the simpliямБed orthotropic plates are used, is only used is as boundary
for the more important level 3 model, the time needed to do all these tests must be taken
into consideration. Also, it is noted that the three orthotropic plate types, that have not been
tested, are more indirectly connected to the level 3 model than TP1. Most important for the
ямБnal model is the strong-axis stretching and bending, which from the tests presented in Table
4.6 showed relatively good behavior. Based on the three reasons presented, the analytically
calculated stiямАnesses are used for the inclined web plate, the bottom plate, the walkway plate
and the part of the top plate 2 which is not included in the level 3 model.
==PAGE:47==
4.3
Diaphragm Beam
33
4.3
Diaphragm Beam
The diaphragm beams are modeled with beam elements in the level 2 model, and properties
of the beams need to be found. First, analytical approximate stiямАness parameters are found.
Second, a ямБnite element model is made of the beam and tested to ямБnd diямАerences to the analytical
solutions. As for the orthotropic plate it is time consuming to make the tests, thus, only one
beam is tested. As the properties of the beams are much simpler than those of the orthotropic
plates, it has been assessed that the analytical results of the non-tested beams can be improved
by multiplying the properties with correction factors found from the test beam. The beam used
to calculate the correction factors is the top beam, which also is the beam of interest in the later
part of this report and directly connected to the level 3 model. As the beam interacts with the
orthotropic plate it is important to ensure a correct behavior of the connected model. Therefore,
a connected model is investigated and compared to a simple model in section 4.5.1.
4.3.1
Analytical solution
In the approximate solutions for the stiямАness parameters of the diaphragm beams, the spikes
are neglected as their inямВuence would be too complicated to analyze analytically. Instead the
inямВuence is investigated with the test beam model.
The analytical solution of the three types of diaphragm beams, the top beam (TB), the inclined
web beam (IWB), the bottom beam (BB) and the walkway beam (WWB), can be found in
Appendix H. The analytically calculated stiямАness parameters are listed in Table 4.7.
4.3.2
Test beam model
The Abaqus test model includes 15 rib cut-outs, whereby the beam is 9m long. In order to
ensure simple supports without locking any motion, 6 DOFs are constrained. 5 translational
and 1 rotational DOFs are chosen as seen in the set-up ямБgures shown in the following sections.
All loads are applied in the analytically calculated elastic center to ensure minimal coupling
eямАects. All eventual coupling eямАects are ignored. The location of the elastic center was found in
the analytical solution in Appendix H. As it is noted in the following section, the analytically
calculated elastic center is found adequate with no need to be found numerically.

force of 106N is applied at each end. It is noted that the forces are in equilibrium. A look on the
reactions of the deformed model conямБrms this. The deformed beam is seen in Figure 4.24.

4.3.7
VeriямБcation
In order to verify the results found for the diaphragm top beam a small test is made.
A
uniform load of magnitude 106N/m is applied to a beam with clamped and simply supports.
The deformed beams are seen in Figure 4.29 while the results are shown in Table 4.9.
It is found that the simple beam model overestimates the deямВection a bit having clamped sup-
ports while it underestimates the deямВection having simply supports. In both cases the diямАerences
are small and accepted. It is noted that the analytically calculated shear stiямАness used in the
simple model seems to be adequate.

4.4
Connections
As explained in previous sections the Izmit Bay Bridge is modeled on three diямАerent levels, each
consisting of many parts. All the diямАerent parts and the diямАerent levels are to be connected,
and care must be taken in order not to introduce disturbance or extra stiямАness. In the following
considerations and methods used for each connection in the model are presented.
The level 3 model is made of three parts; the webs and the ямВanges of the diaphragm beams
and the orthotropic plate including the deck plate and the ribs as shown in Figure 4.5. All
internal connections in the level 3 model are made by use of a tie constrain, whereby all DOFs
are constrained so that the connections act as welds.
The level 2 model consists of тАЩorthotropic plateтАЩ shell elements, diaphragm beam elements and
internal rod beam elements. The level 2 model consisting of ямБve deck segments is shown in
Figure 4.4. All internal level 2 connections are made as tie constrains, while caution is needed
for the connections between the level 3 model and the level 2 model.
The longitudinal sides of the level 3 model is simply connected to the shell elements by use of tie
constrains (C1 in Figure 4.30). It is noted that the тАЩorthotropic plateтАЩ shell element is referred
to the deck plate so that the forces interact in the correct out-of-plane location.
The ends of the level 3 model (C2 in Figure 4.30) is also connected by use of tie constrains, but
in order to connect the ribs to the shells as well, the ends must be considered as cross sections.
This is done by use of a position tolerance larger than the rib height without adjusting the initial
position of the slave nodes. Hereby several nodes of the level 3 model is connected to each node
of the level 2 model as seen in Figure 4.31a.

Figure 4.32: Level 1 model connections.
The same principle is used for the connection between the level 2 internal rod beams and the
level 3 diaphragm beams. In reality this connection is made by use of a gusset plate in order
to distribute the forces, but as this connection is of no interest in the following investigation,
the internal rod beams are connected to the diaphragm beam ямВanges. In order to distribute the
forces similarly to what a gusset plate would do, a position tolerance identical to the diameter of
the internal rod is used without adjusting the initial position of the slave nodes. This connection
can be seen in Figure 4.31b.
The level 2 and level 3 model diaphragm beams are connected by use of a coupling constrain as
seen in Figure 4.31c. All DOFs are constrained whereby the ends of the level 3 model diaphragm
beam act as cross sections.
The level 1 model consists of the pylons made as one part including cross beams, the main
cables, the hangers and the deck as seen in Figure 4.2. All internal level 1 connections are made
by use of Connector Assignments, whereby wires are deямБned in between all connected points.
The connector sections are beams, thus, all DOFs are connected. The connections between the
towers and the main cables and the main cables and the hangers are seen in Figure 4.32. The
connections between the hangers and the deck are a bit more complicated as the wires simulates
the width of the deck to get the correct overall torsional stiямАness. In the beginning of the project
a test was made thereof as seen in Appendix I. The wires can be seen in Figure 4.32.
The ends of the level 2 model in form of shell elements are connected to the deck beam elements
of the level 1 model by use of coupling constrains so that the ends act as cross sections. This is
seen in Figure 4.32 and also initially tested in Appendix I.

4.5
Model testing
In the following, tests are made on connected parts of the model.
4.5.1
Orthotropic plate - diaphragm beam interaction
The diaphragm beams are connected to the orthotropic plates, whereby the orthotropic plates
act as top ямВanges. A level 3 model is compared to a level 2 model to ensure that the level 2
model produces correct results. The test plate model presented in section 4.2.2 is combined with
the test beam model presented in section 4.3.2, so that the stiямАness in the beam direction can
be tested. Firstly, the bending stiямАness is tested by applying a moment after which a uniform
load is applied to check for shear deformations.
Bending stiямАness
The test set-up of the level 3 model can be seen in Figure 4.33a.
The plate model is con-
nected to the beam model by Tie constraint, whereby all DOFs are constrained. The test set-up
of the level 2 model can be seen in Figure 4.33b. The plate is made with a general stiямАness ma-
trix section with parameters as presented in section 4.2.10, while the the beam is made with a
generalized proямБle with parameters as presented in section 4.3.6. It is noted that an appropriate
elastic center is deямБned for the beam so that the beam element can be placed in the plane of the
plate.
A moment of magnitude 106Nm is applied at each end of the model. As for previous tests only 6
DOFs are constrained to ensure free deformations. The deformed models can be seen in Figure

Uniform load
The second test of the interaction between the orthotropic plate and the diaphragm beams
is a uniform load applied to the top of the plate with supports at the ends of the beam. By not
supporting the plate, the model is closer to the ямБnal situation. It is then to be decided whether
the beam ends should act as cross sections or if the DOFs without constrains should be aloud
to deform freely. In reality the solution is somewhere in between as the beams are continuous.
In Figure 4.35 a result of the two options can be seen. From the ямБgure the importance of how
the ends act is clearly seen, where stiямАness is added when the end acts as a cross section. In the
ямБnal model the diaphragm beams of the level 3 model are connected to the beam of the level 2
model rigidly, so that the end of level 3 model beam will act as a cross section. Therefore, the
stiямАer option of the two is used.
In order for the ends of the diaphragm beam to act as a cross section the end nodes are connected
to reference points by use of coupling constrains, constraining all translational DOFs.
Two
types of boundary conditions can be introduced on the reference points. The ямБrst being simple
supports locking all translational DOFs and the second being clamped supports locking the
rotational DOFs as well. Both situations are considered for the uniform load test.
As for the bending stiямАness test, two models are made, a level 3 model and a level 2 model. The
geometry are the same as for the models shown in Figure 4.33, while the boundary conditions are
as explained above. The uniform load is applied to the top plate with a magnitude of 106N/m2.
The results can be seen in Figure 4.36 and Table 4.12, where the maximum deямВections in the
beams are compared. The diямАerences are with respect to the level 3 model.
It is found that the performance of the simpliямБed level 2 model is inadequate. By looking at the
beam itself, as seen in Figure 4.37, it becomes clear that the diямАerence is in the beam, while the
Figure 4.35: Shear strains in the diaphragm beam due to uniform load. Left end; unconstrained DOFs
acting freely, right end; acting as a single cross section.

plates performs adequately. All previous tests involving the beam showed a diямАerence of of up

iteration, it is found that the strong-axis shear stiямАness should be 60% smaller than what was
found analytically, hereby Ky = 2.7 ┬╖ 108N. By using the adjusted shear stiямАness the results
seen in Figure 4.38 and 4.39 and Table 4.13 are found. The diямАerences are with respect to the
level 3 model.
As seen from Table 4.13 a small diямАerence is found when using clamped supports, thus, the new
shear stiямАness seems adequate.

4.5.2
Total deck
By connecting all diaphragm beams and internal rods to the orthotropic plates a total deck
model is made. To test the validity of the total deck, its properties when acting as a beam are
tested and compared to those found by COWI as shown in Table 3.1. A test model is made
of ямБve deck segments giving a total length of 125m. The deck is supported in a way so that
6 DOFs are constrained, 5 translational and 1 rotational, ensuring the deck to deform freely.
The test loads are applied in reference points being located at the elastic center. The location
of the elastic center was found by applying a normal force and then calculating the distance to
the elastic center by use of the bending stiямАness. The elastic center was found to be located
1.762m below the top center point.
The reference points are connected to the deck section
by use of a coupling constrain. Under stretching and bending only longitudinal deformation is
constrained (U1, see Figure 4.41), while the vertical deformation is constrained under twisting
(U2, see Figure 4.41). Measurement points are located at a distance 25m from each end in
order to evade local disturbance form the load application. The boundary conditions and load
application under stretching can be seen in Figure 4.41.
The tests are made similar to those of the diaphragm beam in section 4.3, thus not much
introduction and explaining are made. The ямБrst test is stretching. A load of magnitude 109N is
applied at the reference points in each end of the total deck. The deformed deck is seen in Figure
4.42a. For the two bending tests a moment of 109Nm is applied at each end. The deformed deck
due to bending can be seen in Figure 4.42b and 4.42c for weak-axial and strong-axial bending,
respectively. The last test is twisting, which is introduced by a torsional moment of magnitude
109Nm at each end. The deformed deck can be seen in Figure 4.42d.
From the elongation of the deck in the stretching test, the area can be found, while the moment
of inertia about each axis and the torsional constant are found from the relevant rotations. The
properties given by COWI in comparison with the properties found from the tests are shown in

4.6
Meshing
Meshing of a ямБnite element model is of great importance. It is necessary to establish a reямБned
mesh to ensure converged results as well as it is important to ensure good transitions in between
diямАerent parts and within a part when downsizing is needed. A reямБned mesh is ensured by
checking whether the results have converged as it was seen for the orthotropic plate in Appendix
G. Multiple times throughout the work with the ямБnite element model it has been checked whether
the results have converged. The mesh presented in the following section have shown to give
converged results to an acceptable degree. Beside ensuring converged results the mesh can be
veriямБed by Abaqus to ensure that the shape of the elements are acceptable using multiple criteria.
All elements meet the default criteria.
The level 1 model is meshed coarsely, since it mainly constitutes as boundary for the level 2
model. Each deck part and cable part between hangers are made of 3 quadratic beam elements
(B32) while hangers are made of at least 3 elements (B32). The level 1 model constitutes of
2050 elements.
The general mesh seed of the level 2 model is made by a size control of 1m, while a smooth
transition is made near the level 3 model. The level 2 model mesh is shown in Figure 4.43.
Quadratic shell elements (S8R) are used. The level 2 model constitutes of 15978 elements.
The mesh of the level 3 model is made with a general size control of 150mm. As the middle
diaphragm is central for the investigation, the mesh is smoothly transitioned to a mesh size of
Figure 4.43: Level 2 model mesh.

4.7
Base state
A base state is deямБned, taking into account the overall stiямАness of the bridge from the cables and
hangers being pre-tensioned. The pre-tensioning ensures the geometry of the bridge deck under
dead load. All results presented of the full non-linearly calculated bridge will be with respect to
this base state.
Boundary conditions
The boundary conditions of the Izmit Bay Bridge are shown in Figure 4.45, 4.46 and 4.47.
The pylons are supported on all translational and two rotational DOFs ensuring ямБxed supports.
The lateral displacement of the deck is restrained at the pylons, thus, a boundary condition is
introduced on the deck. The ends of the deck are supported longitudinally which in general is
not the case as expansions joints are used. But since fatigue is investigated, the loading is fast
moving and a pair of hydraulic buямАers ensure restraining ([31] clause 3.1.2).
Self weight and Pre-tensioning
As mentioned previously the self weight and pre-tensioning are made consistently with the
IBB2012-model [27]. For cables, hangers and the pylons, the dead load simply follows from the
density of steel, while surfacing must be added to the density of the top deck plates. All adjusted
densities and the selected distribution of the self weight of the deck are described and calculated
in Appendix E. The pre-tensioning of the cables and hangers is applied as bolt loads as shown
in Figure 4.48. The adjusted lengths of all cables and hangers are listed in Appendix D.
Results
A non-linear calculation was made of the Izmit Bay Bridge model. The deformed model consti-
tuting the base state is shown in Figure 4.49. Small resulting deямВections are seen of the deck,
similarly to what was seen for the IBB2012-model in Figure 4.3b. Naturally these deямВections
and the variation in deямВection introduce stresses in the deck. These stresses are simply part of
the base state and will not be a part of the results in the subsequent sections.

==PAGE:63==
5
Fatigue
Fatigue is a physical phenomena where cracks form and propagate through a material under
cyclic loading [32]. The cracks mainly propagate in locations of high local tensile stress, known
as stress raisers or hot spots. Especially weld toes are sensitive to fatigue due to the high tensile
residual stress imposed by the welding process.
The fatigue cracks mainly initiates from the intrusion of the slip planes, caused by the dislocation
movement, serving as stress raisers [33]. The fatigue process is often deямБned as a three phase
process which is illustrated in Figure 5.1 and presented as follows.
Crack initiation: At this stage small cracks have initiated but only extends over a few grain
sizes. The cracks propagates slightly diagonal with respect to the normal plane, relative to the
material surface.
Crack propagation: At the crack propagation stage, a dominate crack starts to develop and
propagate normal to the material surface. The rate of crack propagation increases as can be
described by the Paris Law [33].
Final fracture: At this stage the crack is large enough to cause ямБnal fracture of the structure.
This corresponds to the dominating crack being large enough to exceed the critical crack length
deямБned by the material toughness.
In order to asses the fatigue life, the local stress ranges are to be compared against an allowable
stress range.
In section 5.1 methods of calculating the local stress range is presented.
The
assessment of the stresses can be done using several methods as explained in section 5.2. The
allowable stress ranges are deямБned trough detail classiямБcations with associated S-N curves. The
Figure 5.1: The fatigue process can be described by three phases; crack initiation (phase 1), crack
propagation (phase 2) and ямБnale fracture (phase 3).
==PAGE:64==
50
5
Fatigue
Figure 5.2: Stress deямБnitions for welded attachments (ref.: [34]).
S-N curves were found from extensive test data, and reduced properly in order to ensure an
acceptable probability of failure. The use of detail categories and S-N curves for the details
investigated in this report are described in section 5.2.2.
5.1
Local stress ranges
The local stress range used for fatigue life assessment must be compatible with the assumptions
made in deriving the detail categories and the S-N curves. Three diямАerent deямБnitions of the
stress ranges are generally used. Each one of them have limitations and place requirements on
the FE modeling assumptions:
тАв Nominal or modiямБed (local) nominal stress
тАв Geometric (hot spot, structural) stress
тАв EямАective notch stress (notch stress, total stress)
The diямАerences between the three stress deямБnitions are depicted in Figure 5.2. In the following
the three stress range deямБnitions are elaborated.
5.1.1
Nominal or modiямБed (local) nominal stress
The nominal stress is the stress calculated in the sectional area under consideration, using
simple beam theory, neglecting the local stress raising eямАects of the welded joint. If the stress
raising eямАects of the macro-geometric shape of the cross section (also called the gross stress
==PAGE:65==
5.1
Local stress ranges
51
Figure 5.3: DeямБnition of structural hot spot stress (ref.:
[35]).
Figure 5.4: IIW extrapolation method
(ref.: [35]).
concentrations) are included, the stress is called a modiямБed (local) nominal stress.
In this
project a macro-geometric eямАect is e.g. the cut-outs in the diaphragm beams. Overall elastic
behavior is assumed.
5.1.2
Geometric (hot spot, structural) stress
The hot spot stress approach for fatigue assessment is based on ямБnite element modeling in which
the geometric local (hot spot) stress is calculated. The hot spot stress is deямБned at the toe of the
weld and includes both gross and local stress concentrations, but not the stress concentration
(non-linear stress component) at the weld toe which occurs due to the local stiямАness and the
geometry of the weld. When the hot spot stress is determined by linear FE-analysis the local
stress at the location of the hot spot corresponds to a singular point in which the stress ямБeld,
according to theory of elasticity, will not be deямБned. For this reason certain methods have been
developed to approximate the hot spot stress. One of these methods is linear extrapolation
which have been used in the current analysis in accordance with IIW recommendations, [35]
clause 2.2.3. The linearization is explained in Figure 5.3.
The reference points used for extrapolation depends on the meshing and the plate thickness. As
a ямБne mesh is used, as presented in section 4.6, the points, in which the local transverse stress
should be determined, are presented in Figure 5.4.
The hot spot stress is then found by extrapolation of the stress variation to the weld toe assuming
a linear stress variation as seen in Eq. (13).
╧ГHS = 1.67╧Г0.4t тИТ0.67╧Г1.0t
(13)
5.1.3
EямАective notch stress
The eямАective notch stress is found through an extremely ямБne volumetric FE model which includes
the geometry of the weld, whereby the local stress concentrations, which arise at the weld toe,
are included in the stress calculation. Fatigue failures rising from the weld root can be assessed
with this method.
The eямАective notch stress is then compared to a single fatigue resistant
strength, typically around 225MPa [34]. As a very detailed model is needed, the eямАective notch
stress is rarely used for practical purposes and not included in the Eurocode [36]. It is noted
==PAGE:66==
52
5
Fatigue
that the stress range at the weld toe in the parenting metal still needs to be assessed with the
hot-spot stress as well, as misalignment needs to be explicitly modeled. However, the eямАective
notch stress is well suited to compare the eямАects of diямАerent weld geometries.
5.2
Structural VeriямБcation Method
The stress ranges found for a detail is then used to assess the fatigue damage done by the load.
Several methods exist, but as the actual fatigue damage is not within of the scope of this report,
only the "damage accumulation method" is presented to give understanding of the structural
veriямБcation used for the deck of Izmit Bay Bridge [34]. Solely equations and S-N curves used for
the presented details are shown.
5.2.1
Damage accumulation method
The damage accumulation method considers the actual stress history of the detail, so that
an economic solution can be found without conservative considerations. The failure criterion
can be deямБned by the Palmgren MinerтАЩs summation rule [33]. This summation rule deямБnes an
accumulated damage, Dd, based on number of cycles at a given level of stress range with respect
to the number of cycles to failure estimated by the corresponding S-N curve. The equation
ensuring adequate fatigue life by use of Palmgren MinerтАЩs rule is as follows.

5.2.2
S-N curves
In order to deямБne the fatigue endurance, the relation between the applied load or state of stress
needs to be related to the resistance or endurance of the structure relative to fatigue crack
growth. One way to establish this relationship is the calculation model called the S-N curves
presented in Eurocode 3 [36]. This model is deямБned by a log-log-linear regression model based
on empirical test data which relates the cyclic nominal, or hot spot, stress range to an endurance
deямБned by number of cycles to failure, deямБned for a probability of failure of 5 percent. The S-N
curves for cyclic nominal stress from Eurocode 3 are presented in Figure 5.5.

5.3
Method Adopted for the Investigation
For the details investigated in this report, nominal detail categories have been used in the struc-
tural veriямБcation made by COWI [34] even though geometric (hot-spot) detail classiямБcations are
given in EN 1993-1-9 Annex B. Several reasons are presented in [34] clause 3.3.4. Most impor-
tant is the fact that EN 1993-1-9 Annex B does not cover eямАects of misalignment, thus eventual
misalignments from fabrication must be modeled and taken into consideration. Naturally, this
is not ideal and very time consuming, thus, a conservative choice was made of using the nom-
inal detail categories for the COWI design veriямБcation. The relevant nominal detail categories
for detail 1a and 6 (see Figure 6.1 and 6.2, respectively) are shown in Figure 5.6a and 5.6b,
respectively.
However, the nominal stress approach is not very suitable for an investigation where several
designs are to be evaluated. It is assessed that the hot-spot stress approach gives a better basis
for comparison as solely stresses will be compared.
5.4
Load Placement Using InямВuence Lines and Surfaces
Even small deviations in the load placement can have great inямВuence on the stress ranges of the
details subjected to fatigue. It is therefore important to identify the worst possible position of the
load. An easy way to identify that exact position is by use of inямВuence surfaces. A introduction
to the use thereof and BettiтАЩs Theorem, which is the essential theorem on the subject, is given
in the subsequent section. First, a small walk-through of the procedure typically used to deal
with load placement in fatigue assessment of bridges is presented. The load model used for the
present investigation is elaborated in section 6.1.
In fatigue evaluations, typically, inямВuence lines are made for the longitudinally load placement
[29, 34]. Unit axle inямВuence lines of the diямАerent axle types are then combined to calculate the
vehicle local stress history, so that the worst longitudinally load placement can be identiямБed. The
unit axle inямВuence lines are typically made by moving a unit axle load in small increments, calcu-
lating the local stress of the detail of interest at every increment. When approaching the location
of interest the increment size is reduced. This method has huge computational requirements as
many repetitive calculations are required, which eventually can lead to a oversimpliямБed model
and a less reямБned mesh in order to minimize the computational cost for each increment.
For the transverse axle (load) placement a more simple approach is used. The Eurocode (EN
1991-2 [37]) prescribes a transverse axle position distribution with ямБve bins. The distribution
is seen in Figure 5.7. Longitudinal inямВuence lines can be found with transverse increments of
0.1m. Typically, longitudinal inямВuence lines are found at 7 transverse locations in increments
of 0.1m around the center of the lane [34]. The highest stress range is then used as the 50%
frequency to make the fatigue evaluation conservative.
As it will be seen in the structural analysis in section 6, the local stress range can be sensitive
to a very small area. Hereby, only a single set of wheels has impact, and the local stress range
is very sensitive to the transverse position of the load. This is not assessed very well by the
method explained above as a transverse load incrementation of 0.1m is quite rough when having
0.3m in between each rib wall.
==PAGE:69==
5.4
Load Placement Using InямВuence Lines and Surfaces
55
Figure 5.7: Frequency distribution of transverse location of center line of vehicle (ref.: [37]).
Another approach to ямБnd the maximum stress range susceptible to a local detail is by use of
inямВuence surfaces. InямВuence surfaces gives a neat graphical representation on where to place
load in order to introduce the maximum stress at the selected detail. In the following section
an introduction to BettiтАЩs theorem and inямВuence surfaces used with ямБnite element analysis is
given.
5.4.1
BettiтАЩs theorem
The theoretical basis of inямВuence lines and inямВuence surfaces is BettiтАЩs theorem, also known as
Maxwell-Betti reciprocal work theorem. The theorem is valid for linear elastic structures only.

element analysis. Reference is made to [38].

As mentioned a linear elastic material is assumed, thus, HookeтАЩs law is in place; ╧ГP

n

(21)
The theorem says that; the sum of the product of forces of the P system and the displacements
at the corresponding coordinates caused by the Q system is equal to the sum of the products
of the forces of the Q system and the displacements at the corresponding coordinates caused by
the P system.
Now assume that there is only one force F P
i
= 1 in the P system acting on coordinate i, and
one force F Q
j = 1 in the Q system acting at j. Eq. (21) is then reduced to
dQ
i
= dP
j
(22)
Eq. (22) can be written in the form
fij = fji
(23)
where fij is the displacement at i due to a unit force at j, and fji is the displacement at j
due to a unit force at i. The displacements in Eq. (23) are the ямВexibility coeямГcients [39]. The
equation is called the Maxwell-Betti reciprocal work theorem and says that; The displacement
at coordinate i due to a unit force at coordinate j is equal to the displacement at j due to a
unit force acting at i.
The extension to FE-analysis can be done by use of GreenтАЩs second identity as shown in Hart-
mann [40] clause 3.3.1. Hereby the displacement at node i due to a unit force at node j in the
ямБnite element model is equal to the displacement at node j due to a unit force acting at node
i.
As it will be seen in section 6.3, the theorem is used by applying two opposing forces at two
neighboring nodes resulting in a certain stress in a single element. Then the displacements in
the structure show the most critical location for the load placement. As vehicular loads solely
acts on the deck plate, the displacements of the deck plate due to the force couple can be used
as the inямВuence surface for the loaded element.
==PAGE:71==
6
Structural Analysis
A structural analysis of the existing design of the Izmit Bay Bridge with focus on fatigue is
performed based on the ямБnite element model presented in section 4 and the methods described
in section 5.
In a real design many details must be investigated for fatigue. This has already been done for
the Izmit Bay Bridge. The results of a COWI investigation are used to simplify the present
investigation, only to include the most important and exposed details. Following the COWI
fatigue design report [34], two details experience the most fatigue damage. Damage results from
the COWI design report can be seen in Appendix J. The ямБrst detail, referred to as detail 1a,
is located at the weld toe of the partial penetration weld between the orthotropic deck top
plate and the ribs. An illustration of the potential fatigue crack in the top plate can be seen in
Figure 6.1. The second detail, referred to as detail 6, is the cut-out in the web of the diaphragm
beams. Huge stress concentrations occur at the cut-out, thus, its geometry is of great importance
[15, 16]. The stress tangential to the edge of the cut-out, introducing fatigue cracks, is illustrated
in Figure 6.2.
In the following, the speciямБc location of a detail is referred to in terms of a rib number (R1-R8)
as seen in Figure 6.3. For detail 1a, the side of the rib is referred to as a and b for the outer
most and the center most side of the rib, respectively.
As detail 1a is located at the bottom of the top plate, the shell elements used for the top plate
are referred to by the bottom of the element. Hereby, all stresses shown for the top plate are for
the bottom surface of the plate. This means that beside including membrane stresses, stresses
rising from bending over the rib wall are included in the hot-spot stress. This also applies for
the inямВuence surfaces in section 6.3, as the forces applied at the hot-spot, must be applied at
the bottom of the element.
