6.1
Fatigue Load Model
Five fatigue load models are accepted within the Eurocode ([37] clause 4.6) for evaluating fatigue.
In the following, only the one used in the structural analysis is presented.
Fatigue Load Model 4 (FLM4) is the most extensive model without using actual recorded traямГc
data. Hereby, the most economical solution can be found as the model is not as conservative as
FLM1, FLM2 and FLM3 [37]. FLM4 was used in the original design, [34], thus adopted for this
analysis. FLM4 consists of 23 diямАerent vehicle types, each being built up from a combination
of ямБve diямАerent axle types. Each axle consists of between two and six patch wheel loads, each
measuring 320 ├Ч 220 mm at pavement level.
In a full fatigue evaluation all combinations of vehicles should be tested to ямБnd the total accu-
mulated damage as explained in section 5.2.1. The repetitiveness of the full exercise is too time
consuming, thus, previous results are used to identify the speciямБc load with the most inямВuence.
As mentioned, two details are of interest, detail 1a and 6. For both details one vehicle type
was found to have the most impact: vehicle 5A-H [29, 34]. A combination of a huge load on a
standard axle together with a large share of all passing vehicles made it so that more than half of
the fatigue damage experienced by the two details of interest was caused by vehicle 5A-H. The
accumulated damage results for detail 1a and 6, made by COWI [34], are shown in Appendix
J.
Vehicle 5A-H consists of 5 axles; 1 steering axle and 4 standard axles. The largest load of 100kN
is placed on the second axle. The geometric conямБguration of vehicle 5A-H with the magnitude
of the load on each axle is shown in Figure 6.4.
According to the Eurocode ([37] clause 4.3.6) the dispersal through the pavement and the or-
thotropic deck can be taken at a spread-to-depth ratio of 1 horizontally to 1 vertically down to
the middle plane of the top deck plate. Hereby, the surfaces, to which the load is applied, is
increased. The principle can be seen in Figure 6.5. 60 mm pavement is used. As the thickness of
the top deck plate is 14mm, the dispersal allows for an extra length and width of the load surface
of 2 ├Ч 67 mm. Ultimately, this leads to an overlap of the load surfaces of the two neighboring
wheels in each side of axle type 2. A simpliямБcation has been made by allowing the load to be
distributed over a single surface representing both wheels.
==PAGE:73==
6.2
Initial Vehicle Position
59
Figure 6.4: Geometric conямБguration of vehicle 5A-H with load magnitudes.
Figure 6.5: Dispersal of wheel loads through pavement and top deck plate (ref.: [37]).
6.1.1
FEM implementation
The application of the vehicle load in Abaqus can be done in several ways. As the load is due
to gravity, it is applied vertically. Due to the inclination of the deck a surface traction load is
used so that a load direction vector can be chosen. The surface traction load is applied to a
surface, thus, a surface must be deямБned. In order to deямБne the surface directly on the deck plate
partitions must be made to deямБne the boundaries of the load surface. Hereby, the mesh needs
to be recalculated every time the location of the load is changed, also it will be troublesome
to deямБne several closely spaced loads at the same time. Instead a new structural part is made,
deямБning the vehicle by use of ямВexible shell elements.
The ямВexibility is deямБned through the
thickness and YoungтАЩs modulus so that the applied surface traction is transferred directly to the
deck of the bridge without adding any stiямАness. The vehicle shell elements are connected to the
shell elements of the deck plate trough a tie constraint. By having made a speciямБc part for the
vehicle, it is easily moved around to tests several locations of the load.
6.2
Initial Vehicle Position
Initially, critical stress locations of detail 1a and 6 of the orthotropic deck are found. The vehicle
is placed in the center of the lane. The results are used to produce inямВuence surfaces in the
following section.
==PAGE:74==
60
6
Structural Analysis
The initial longitudinal position of the vehicle is based on ямБndings found by COWI. According
to the COWI design report ([34] clause 6.1), the critical location for detail 1a was found to be
located 0.4m from the diaphragm. Also, it was found that axle 2 had the most inямВuence due
to a bigger axle load. Therefore, initially, the longitudinal center of axle B is placed 0.4m from
the diaphragm. As it will be seen from the inямВuence surfaces in the following section, detail 6 is
aямАected by a larger area. Thus, having axle C, D and E centered above a diaphragm is found to
produce the largest stress at the web cut-outs according to the COWI design report ([34] clause
6.8).
6.2.1
Detail 1a
The hot-spot stress, used for comparison, of detail 1a is found through linearization of the
transverse stress at a distance 0.4t and 1.0t from the weld toe as explained in section 5.1.2.
As a partial penetration weld is used, the weld toe is assumed to be at the edge of the web.
Therefore, in the ямБnite element model, half of the rib wall thickness is added to the distance
from the connecting node.
The transverse stresses in the orthotropic plate, due to vehicle 5A-H being placed in the center
of the lane, are shown in Figure 6.6. Four locations are found to be critical, two under each set
of wheels. The stresses at the linearization points of those four locations are shown in Table 6.1
together with the calculated hot-spot stresses.
6.2.2
Detail 6
The critical stress at detail 6, the cut-outs of the web, is found as the maximum principal
stress along the cut-out. As explained in section 4.6, the meshing is reямБned so that the result
has converged to an acceptable degree, as it was done for the orthotropic plate in Appendix
G.
The principle stresses from axle C-E being centered over the diaphragm are shown in Figure 6.7.
The largest principle stress is found in the cut-out under rib 7 to be

The hot-spot stress at detail 1a is negative, thus, in order to produce the inямВuence surface,
two opposing forces are applied in neighboring nodes creating a negative stress. The forces are
applied transversely aligned with the deck plate at the bottom of the plate. The transverse
stresses at location R4a can be seen in Figure 6.8. Following BettiтАЩs theorem (section 5.4.1) the
inямВuence surface is simply the resulting deformations. As the vehicle load is applied vertically,
only the vertical deformations are of interest. The vertical deformations can be seen in Figure
6.9. Negative deямВection indicates where to apply the load in order to produce a negative hot-spot
stress.
A very small area is found to inямВuence the detail 1a hot-spot stress at location R4a. The same
applies for the three other locations shown in Appendix K. It is seen that applying load at the
exact location results in a positive hot-spot stress, while everything else applied within 0.3m
gives a negative hot-spot stress. This shows that a wheel centered above the hot-spot will result
in the worst possible stress. Also, it is seen that load applied at a transverse distance of 0.3-0.6m
from the hot-spot reduces the hot-spot stress.

6.3.2
Detail 6
The second detail of interest is the web cut-out. The initial placement of a vehicle in the center
of the lane showed that the negative stress in one side of a particular cut-out was the most
critical. Thus, only one detail 6 location (R7) is investigated further. The highest stress was
found in the bottom of the side of the cut-out, thus, an opposing force couple is applied at the
bottom of the linear part of the cut-out. By using the linear part, the forces are ensured to have
opposite direction without introducing any twist. The principle stresses can be seen in Figure
6.10. The resulting vertical deformations in the top deck plates in the bridge, which constitutes
the inямВuence surface for detail 6 following BettiтАЩs theorem, are shown in Figure 6.11.
From the inямВuence surface it is found that a much larger area of the deck aямАects the stress at
the cut-out than what was found for detail 1a. It is seen that all load in the lane within 5m of
the diaphragm aямАect the stress. Placing the load further to center of the bridge than the cut-out
of interest will reduce the stress. Also, is it clearly seen why the most critical is to center axle
C-E above the diaphragm, as all three axles will aямАect the stress.

6.4
Worst Case
By use of the inямВuence surfaces the worst possible scenario in terms of stress ranges can be
found. In the following detail 1a and 6 are examined.
6.4.1
Detail 1a
A very simple inямВuence surface was found for detail 1a, regardless of the location, showing that
the load simply must be centered above the hot-spot to produce the highest stress. In Figure 6.12
the transverse stresses due to a lane centered vehicle is compared to the wheel being centered
above hot-spot R4a and R3b. The interpolation stresses together with the calculated hot-spot
stresses of all four hot-spot locations are shown i Table 6.2.
It is found that the hot-spot stress is increased with up to 30% compared to stresses from a lane
centered vehicle.
6.4.2
Detail 6
The inямВuence surface for detail 6 showed that all load placed within 5m inямВuence the stress
range of interest. Therefore, it is easily seen why the rear 3 axles of vehicle 5A-H produce the
largest stress range. By use of the inямВuence surface it is found that moving the vehicle 50mm
towards the center of the bridge will increase the stress range. The result is seen in Figure 6.13.
The maximum principle stress is increased by 2.3%, so that the maximum stress range produced
at detail 6 by vehicle 5A-H is found to be
╧Г = тИТ81.28MPa
6.5
Model SimpliямБcations Considerations
Detail 1a was found to be inямВuenced by a relatively small area. Hereby, the related hot-spot
stress is less sensitive to the model simpliямБcations made in the level 2 model. This is not the
case for detail 6, as the inямВuence surface showed impact from a larger part of the level 2 model
as seen in Figure 6.10. Hereby, a greater impact on the detail 6 stress range will be seen from
the simpliямБcations of the level 2 model. In particular, the transition from the level 3 model
diaphragm beams to the level 2 model diaphragm beams is of concern. The reason for this is its
in near distance to the calculated stress range.
Huge simpliямБcations were made throughout the FE modeling in section 4. The most questionable
being the simpliямБcation of the orthotropic plates to shell elements and the reduction in the
diaphragm beams transverse shear stiямАnesses. The complex orthotropic plates were simpliямБed
to a symmetric 6 by 6 general stiямАness matrix, while the extra shearing in the spikes of the
diaphragm beams led to iteratively reduced transverse shear stiямАnesses.
The simpliямБcations
were tested with simple models, and relatively good agreements were found. But as it is diямГcult
to conclude anything about the inямВuence in the ямБnal situation, a new model is tested and
compared.
Top plate 1 and the underlying beams adjacent to the level 3 model are now replaced with a
detailed level 3 model. Except for the thickness of the ribs, the geometry is the same as for the

6.6
Model Size and Boundary Conditions Considerations
In an FE-analysis one needs to consider the detailing of the model, boundary conditions, load
applications, etc. The model used for the current analysis was described and argued in section
4. An extensive model was chosen to ensure correct boundary conditions with pre-tensioned
cables and hangers. It is now of interest to see the importance of the extensive model and to see
how the results may be aямАected by choosing a simpler model. Firstly, three alternative models
are presented after which results are compared and discussed.
Alternative 1: No pre-tensioning
The ямБrst alternative uses the full model presented in section 4 but without pre-tensioning of the
cables and hangers. Hereby, no non-linear calculations are needed, and the analysis runs much
faster. The non-linear eямАects are usually neglected, thus, it is of interest to see the importance
thereof. Gravity is omitted as well.
==PAGE:81==
6.6
Model Size and Boundary Conditions Considerations
67
Figure 6.15: Alternative 3 model with BCs.
Figure 6.16: Alternative 4 model with BCs.
Alternative 2: Supports at hanger connections
Alternative number 2 is a model where the geometric nonlinear eямАects from applying gravity is
included but the pre-tensioning is neglected. Instead, vertical supports are introduced at the
hanger-to-deck connections. Hereby, solely the non-linear eямАects from gravity are seen, and the
overall stiямАness from the entire bridge with hangers and main cables is neglected.
Alternative 3: SimpliямБed level 2 model
The third alternative is inspired by the model used for the original design veriямБcation [34].
The model consists of the level 3 model and half of a level 2 model segment. The model with
boundary conditions is shown in Figure 6.15.
It is seen that the internal rods are supported in all three directions, adding additional stiямАness.
The outer side of the level 3 model is not connected to anything, whereby the edge is free and
more ямВexible. Only a small part of the diaphragm is modeled, thus, the huge stiямАness from a
closed diaphragm is neglected. The center of the deck is supported transversely to simulate the
other half of the deck, while longitudinal restraint is ensured by constraining one end.
Alternative 4: Level 3 model
Finally, a very simple model is considered, solely based on the level 3 model. The level 3 model
is shown with boundary conditions in Figure 6.16. Several references use a similar simple model
for investigations of orthotropic decks [9, 12, 29]. All diaphragm beams are supported vertically
at both ends, whereby much stiямАness is added to the system.

Comparison

make a situation identical to what is seen in section 6.2. The hot-spot stress for detail 1a and
the stress range for detail 6 are compared to the results of the full non-linear calculated model
in Table 6.4. The diямАerences are with respect to the results found in section 6.2.

Detail 1a is considered at ямБrst. Not including pre-tensioning of the cables and hangers changes
the hot-spot stresses a bit but not signiямБcantly.
Thus, it is seen that the changed overall
stiямАness from the pre-tensioning has little inямВuence on detail 1a. Bigger diямАerences are found for
the second alternative as the stress diямАer with up to 3% by neglecting the overall system of the
bridge, thus, the overall stiямАness aямАects detail 1a even though the inямВuence surfaces showed a

could be made thereof, but as it is not the main objective to ямБnd the worst possible stresses,

==PAGE:84==
==PAGE:85==
7
Alternative Solution
Up to this point of the project, the Izmit Bay Bridge design has been discussed. From studying
the results of the ямБnal design [31, 34], two particularly critical details were found appropriate
for a further study. Through numerical calculations, the advantages and disadvantages of the
current design were revealed. In particular, the exposure to fatigue damages was studied. It is
now of interest to develop a new design with improved fatigue performance. Multiple scenarios,
which change the current system to greater and lesser extents, were discussed in a design process.
A selection of these are presented and discussed in the following. An initial limitation is to keep
the original overall dimensions of the bridge deck, so that a new design can be implemented in
the FE-model presented in section 4. First, a brief discussion of requirements for a new design
is presented, based on the work presented up to this point of the report.
7.1
Requirements
Based on results from in preceding work and from discussions with COWI [25, 41], three prop-
erties subjected to requirements were established;
тАв Overall deck stiямАness
тАв Local stiямАness
тАв Weight
In the following, the requirements for each property are elaborated.
Overall deck stiямАness
The overall deck stiямАness, represented by moments of inertia in Table 3.1, should be considered
when introducing a new system. The current system is optimized, so naturally the total deck
stiямАness is utilized to its maximum at some locations [41]. If the overall dimensions of the box
are maintained, the amount of steel used in the top and the bottom of the deck needs to be
approximately similar to what is used in the current design. For top plate 2 (TP2) it is found
that a steel volume equivalent to a 26.1mm plate should be used (Appendix F). Alternatively
a new box design with a bigger height of the box could compensate for a reduced amount of
steel. However, it is noted that there are size limitations, so that e.g. halving the amount of
steel would be too much.
Local stiямАness
The local stiямАness of the plate spanning between diaphragms has proved to be a big issue for
orthotropic decks due to cracking of surfacing [15]. Problems mainly occur when using too thin
==PAGE:86==
72
7
Alternative Solution
of a top deck plate. Longitudinal cracks appear above ribs as the transverse curvature of the top
plate spanning between diaphragm ribs is too big. The longitudinal ямВexibility of the orthotropic
plate is also of concern as transverse cracks in the surfacing can occur above diaphragms.
The Eurocode prescribes recommended minimum stiямАness values for the stringers in order to
prevent cracking of the surfacing due to diямАerential deямВections. Extrapolating the graph in EN
1993-2 Figure C.4 a minimum stiямАness of approximately 25000cm4 for a stringer is recommended,
when having 5m in between diaphragms [42]. A stringer is deямБned as a rib including deck plate,
thus in the present case the minimum stiямАness is per 600mm. The requirement is equivalent to a
plate stiямАness of 8.75 ├Ч 107Nm. From the analytical calculation of the orthotropic plate located
under the most heavily loaded traямГc lane, the current stiямАness was found to be 9.86 ├Ч 107Nm,
see Appendix F. Thus, the bending stiямАness can be reduced up to 10%. It is noted that the
recommendation is solely for orthotropic decks made of steel, thus the application to other deck
types should be considered, e.g. the inclusion of transverse shear deformations in a sandwich
system.
Weight
As mentioned in the introduction, lower weight is the main reason to use an orthotropic deck
as opposed to other solutions used in smaller bridges, such as concrete decks. Therefore it is
of great importance to consider the weight of a new system. Ideally a lower weight is desired,
but naturally all aspects of a new system should be considered, thus e.g. major reductions in
welding could justify an increased weight.
7.2
Finding an Alternative Solution
Initially two main issues are of concern. The ямБrst is the weight; a lighter system would reduce
costs signiямБcantly as it is not only the amount of steel used in the deck but also in the cables and
hangers. The second issue is the complexity of the detailing, as the orthotropic system includes
a lot of longitudinal welding and complex details at the diaphragms, all subjected to rigorous
inspection.
Weight reductions have always been a concern in the aircraft industry, so much can be gained
by looking in that direction. Over the last many years, and increasingly with the advances in
computing power, topology optimization has been a hot topic. The use in the aircraft industry
has been vital for the increasing eямГciency of modern airplanes.
Trough ямБnite elements the
material layout is optimized with respect to given loads and boundary conditions within a
certain design space [43]. Often this results in organic geometries, which so far have been the
primary obstacles for using topology optimization for larger structures made using traditional
building materials such as steel and concrete. Typically, the application in the aircraft industry
is much smaller, thus the organic shapes can be molded in one piece. This is not possible for a
large bridge deck, thus further research is needed in order to be able to include the additional
limitations of constructing large scale structures. Alternatively the use of more complicated
materials such as elastomer could improve the utilization of topology optimization.
Again,
much innovation and research would be needed. At this point of the current project the use of
topology optimization would be too comprehensive, thus, it is not pursued any further.
Having continuous ribs introduces a lot of welding. Extra care must be taken when dealing with
the deck-to-rib connection of a closed trapezoidal rib as the weld can only be performed from one
side. Orthotropic decks are used to a large extent in Europe. Hereby much experience has been
==PAGE:87==
7.2
Finding an Alternative Solution
73
Figure 7.1: V-truss core steel sandwich panel (ref.: [49]).
gained from the manufacturing procedures, and according to O. S├╕rensen [24] the quality needed
is ensured at decent fabrication costs. In the Unites States the experience is much more limited,
and according to T. Zoli [44] much fewer manufactures are willing to perform the complicated
welds. Therefore an eямАort has been made to discover new procedures that ensures easier welds,
including open ribs [15]. Many diямАerences in perspective are seen in the American AASHTO
Design SpeciямБcations [18] and the European Eurocodes [45], including the use of bulkhead plates
as discussed in the background study (section 2 and [22]). But since the investigated bridge is
being built in Europe, the complications of most concern in Europe are of primary interest.
However, the large number of welds needed is a huge expense, and an alternative solution could
favorably reduce the amount of welding.
Complicated detailing of the present design is also found at the intersection between diaphragms
and ribs. As seen in preceding sections, these details are prone to fatigue, and much processing is
needed to reduce stress concentrations. A system which addresses these complications could be a
sandwich system. From the need for a light-weight plate with improved mechanical performance,
Honeycomb Sandwich Panels were developed for the aircraft industry in the 1940s [46]. The idea
of simply having a light core separating two stronger face plates has been found advantageous
for many applications over the last 50 years.
A sandwich system developed from the current orthotropic deck system is the laser welded steel
sandwich panel (SSP). An example of a SSP with a V-truss core is seen in Figure 7.1. The
system is similar to an orthotropic plate but with an extra bottom plate installed whereby a
stiямАer section can be achieved. In order to keep the weight, or reportedly even save weight
[47], the face plates have to be thin. E.g. for the current design of the Izmit Bay Bridge deck
the top plate should be split into two, resulting in 7mm face plates. Hereby the rib to top
plate connection becomes even more critical. In order to compensate for this, relatively new
laser welding techniques are used, as laser welds have shown to be less prone to fatigue [48].
Also, the use of laser welds automates the welding process, and diямГcult rib-to-deck connections
are avoided as the laser weld is placed in the middle of the V, penetrating through the upper
plate and into the middle of the lower plate. However, laser welding is expensive and yet to be
included in the Eurocodes, as a lot of testing is needed in order to rely on the improved fatigue
performance. One of the biggest problems with the SSP is the low stiямАness of the top plate
resulting in increased diямАerential deямВections between ribs, whereby the issue of cracking of the
surfacing would be even more evident [15].
Another sandwich system developed for heavier loads is the Sandwich Panel System (SPS).
Originally, the company Intelligent Engineering developed the concept together with Elastogran
GmbH to provide impact-resistant structures for the Canadian Arctic oямАshore oil industry [50].
SPS consists of two steel face plates and a continuous polyurethane elastomer core as seen in
Figure 7.2. Initially, the system was used on ships in service e.g. in strengthening vehicle decks
==PAGE:88==
74
7
Alternative Solution
Steel Plate
Polyurethane Core
Steel Plate
Figure 7.2: Sandwich Plate System (ref.: [54]).
(a) The constructed bridge.
 
(b) Deck panel installation.
Figure 7.3: Shenley Bridge, Quebec, Cananda (ref.: [54]).
on ro-ro ferries and repairing impact-damaged cargo spaces in bulk carriers, but soon thereafter
the system was adopted for the design of new ships as well [51].
Along with the extended
use of SPS, internal research found promising properties of the system with regard to bridges
[52].
The SPS was found to be a possible alternative to the bridge deck, thus the system was incor-
porated in the Shenley Bridge in Quebec in 2003 [53]. The SPS was used as an alternative to
heavier concrete deck panels resting on steel girders as seen in Figure 7.3. SigniямБcant research
has been done on the Shenley Bridge in order better to understand the characteristics of the
panels used for bridges [54]. Initially, Intelligent Engineering saw a lot of potential in using the
concept in an alternative solution to orthotropic bridge decks in long span bridges as described
in Kennedy et al. [52]. However, the introduction of SPS in a new long span bridge is still to
come. The reason for this may be the lack of knowledge of the system, thus a lot of testing is
still to be done before a new design can be made with conямБdence.
Meanwhile, the system has gained momentum in bridge repair, as retroямБtting is needed on many
orthotropic decks made with top plates that are too thin [55]. Over the last 10 years the sandwich
system has been tested on older orthotropic steel decks, where 15-30mm polyurethane is spread
out on the existing top plate followed by a new 5-6mm steel top plate, initially reported by
Feldmann et al. [56]. In the Netherlands especially, retroямБtting is a hot topic where more than
85 orthotropic bridge decks are installed [57], and several fatigue problems have been found in
the details investigated in section 6. The properties of two light-weight reinforcing systems have
been tested extensively: the bonded steel plate system and the sandwich plate system [58]. Both
systems showed a good performance and proved to be eямГcient lightweight solutions to refurbish
orthotropic bridge decks and extend their life span. Most interesting is the test made on the
sandwich system, as the test results can be used to evaluate the potential of the SPS for new
bridges.
==PAGE:89==
7.3
Sandwich Plate System
75
Before conducting a literature study, a brainstorm was done on a possible new system. One idea
was a sandwich system similar to the one described above. As the SPS shows somewhat promis-
ing results, it is decided to investigate the possibility of introducing the SPS to long span bridges
as an alternative to the orthotropic plate. Initial ideas on a design using SPS are described in
Kennedy et al. [52], but due to lack of documentation and absence of important result in the
article, the system is still questionable. Therefore, a similar system is investigated.
As the tests of SPS used in retroямБtting show very promising results by having an SPS top plate,
the initial investigation uses SPS in the top plate of the orthotropic plate to stiямАen the top plate
without increasing the weight. Properties of the SPS are introduced further in the following
section.
7.3
Sandwich Plate System
The sandwich plate system consists of two steel face plates and a polyurethane elastomer
core (PU-core). The characteristics of steel are given in Table 3.2 while characteristics of the
polyurethane are given in Table 7.1. The characteristics of the polyurthane are listed as reported
by the manufacturer. Three and four point bending tests of SPS beams were conducted at Delft
University of Technology in 2010 [59] and good agreement of the elastomer characteristics were
found.
One of the biggest concerns using SPS is the temperature dependency of the core material.
As seen from Table 7.1, YoungтАЩs modulus strongly depends on the temperature, as the material
softens with increasing temperature. Going from room temperature to +60тДГhalves the YoungтАЩs
modulus. By increasing the temperature a change in the failure mode is found from the experi-
ments at TU Delft reported in Teixeria de Freitas et al. [59]. For lower temperatures the ямБnal
failure mode is core shear failure, while for specimens tested at +50тДГdelamination at the face to
core interface caused the ямБnal failure. This is due to an increased deformation capacity following
the decreased stiямАness of the PU-core as a result of the high temperature. An FE-model was
compared to the test results, showing good agreement for the lower temperatures with higher
capacities in the tests than what was found by FEA. At +50тДГthe tests showed lower capacities
than the FE-model. This can be explained by the viscoelastic behavior present in the PU-core at
high temperatures, which aямАects the ямВexural behavior of the sandwich beam and decreases the

The fatigue characteristics of the SPS are also of interest. Assuming proper face plate to core

7.3.2
FE modeling
In the following sections, continuum shells (solids) as well as shell elements (SC8R and S8R,
respectively [28]) are used to model the SPS. By using shell elements the layering is deямБned
by 3 integration points in each layer. The shell elements ensure correct shear deformations in
each layer. Approximated shear stresses can be obtained in each layer. By using continuum
shells, each layer is deямБned by a number of elements, leading to the most suямГcient results.
A comparison between the two modeling techniques can be found in Appendix L. Also, the
equivalent properties are investigated. Through a three point bending test up to 9% diямАerence
in deямВection are found between a continuum shell and a shell model. The diямАerence is most
signiямБcant for a beam with clamped supports. The most reliable results are found by using
continuum shells. However, in the following shells elements are used for some investigations,
since the model would be to extensive if continuum shells were used.
7.4
Orthotropic Plate with SPS
The sandwich plate system has been used for retroямБtting of orthotropic bridges over the last 10
years. Polyurethane is spread out on the top deck plate and an additional steel plate is added
on the top. Hereby an eямГcient section is achieved with increased stiямАness. Now, the system is
used in a new design of an orthotropic plate, where the existing top deck plate is replaced by
SPS. To evaluate the system two approaches can be considered. One approach is to maintain
the weight of the top deck plate whereby the stiямАness is increased leading to reduced stresses,
while the other is to optimize the weight to maintain the stresses. The system is assessed by its
ability to reduce the weight of the top deck plate. The complexity of the details is not reduced
but rather increased, as the processing of the top deck plate is increased. Therefore a larger
weight reduction is needed to compensate.
Initially, the weight is maintained. The 14mm top deck plate is replaced by two face plates
of 6mm and a core approximately equal in weight to 2mm steel.
This means that 13.7mm
polyurethane can be used in order to maintain the weight. In the following the core is set to
14mm. This solution is referred to as SPS.6.14.6 (SPS.ttop face plate.tcore.tbottom face plate). The
orthotropic deck with SPS in the top plate is shown in Figure 7.4. Using Eq. (24) the equivalent
bending stiямАness can be found to 2.853 ├Ч 105Nm, which is more than ямБve times the bending
stiямАness of a 14mm steel plate.
In the following, solely detail 1a is considered. The FE-model presented in section 4 is used
for the analysis. The top deck plate of the level 3 model is replaced by the SPS plate. It is
assumed that the existing model without SPS in the deck plate can be used to serve as boundary

Beside fatigue, other important aspects to consider are the curvatures and deямВections in the
plate. As mentioned in the requirements to a new design, longitudinal cracks in the surfacing
have been observed in several orthotropic bridges.
Therefore it is important to look at the
curvature over a rib and the diямАerential deямВections between ribs. The transverse curvature in
the top deck plates due to a lane centered vehicle 5A-H are shown in Figure 7.6. Due to the
increased bending stiямАness of the SPS plates the curvatures are found to be up to 10 times
smaller than for the original 14mm steel plate. Naturally, this is a good property of the SPS,
but as mentioned in the introduction to SPS, large shear deformations can be expected in the
polyurethane as the shear stiямАness is low.
Therefore it is of interest to have a look on the
deямВections.
The diямАerential deямВections of the top deck plate in between two rib walls due to a lane centered
vehicle 5A-H are shown in Figure 7.7. The deямВections are relative to the deямВection at the rib
walls. Although the curvatures were found smaller for the SPS plates, the deямВections are bigger
than for S.14. This is due to shear deformations. Most signiямБcantly it is seen close to the rib wall
as the deямВection is steep. Small curvatures were seen in Figure 7.6, thus, most of the deямВection
is due to shear. Also, it can be observed that the deямВection of S.14 is continuous over the rib
wall as bending governs. It is noted that the deямВection curves for the SPS plates immediately
over the rib walls can be misguiding, as shell elements are used. The top of the SPS plates would
be found to be more continuous if a continuum shell model was used as discussed in Appendix
L.
The ямБrst objective was to reduce the hot-spot stress, which was achieved by an increased bending
stiямАness. Secondly, increased deямВections was found as a result of shear deformations. Limited
diямАerential deямВections are of most importance to prevent cracking in the surfacing but according
to Ove S├╕rensen [24], larger deformations than what is found for a 14mm top deck plate could
be accepted. Based on his experience it is assesses that a 12mm steel plate with ribs placed at a
distance of 300mm would be suямГcient to prevent cracking. Therefore an additional model with
a 12mm top deck plate (S.12) have been tested as seen in Figure 7.7. The deямВections of all three
SPS plates surpasses the deямВection of the S.12 plate. Therefore, it is assessed that none of the
SPS plates would be adequate.

use of 5mm face plates should be assessed, as the welding between the bottom face plate and
the rib is a potential critical detail. For now, these questions are left for further work.

shear modulus. All conямБgurations with a 28mm core was found adequate in terms of stresses and

diямАerential deямВections right in between rib walls. To maintain the weight of the deck plate the

7.5
SPS Deck
Kennedy et al. [52] propose to replace the orthotropic plates in long span bridges with SPS.
According to the article, the system is adequate for long span bridges such as Izmit Bay Bridge.
In preceding work it was found that the sandwich plate system with a polyurethane core is
exposed to large shear deformations.
As discussed, a too large ratio between deformations
caused by shearing and bending can be problematic due to kink-like deformations at supports.
It it therefore of interest to test the system.
Firstly, the behavior of an SPS beam with a proper conямБguration is tested in three point bending.
In section 7.1, requirements to the stiямАness of the deck were presented. The longitudinal bending
stiямАness in the present design is 9.86 ├Ч 107Nm. This is used as a minimum bending stiямАness of
the SPS. Eq. (24) is used to ямБnd a conямБguration which meets the requirement. In the original
design the amount of steel equivalent to a 26mm steel plate is used. An attempt is made to keep
the weight, thus, as for the orthotropic SPS plate, the amount of steel is reduced. The thickness
of the face plates is set to 10mm. To obtain the required bending stiямАness the core needs to be
280mm, and a plate bending stiямАness of 9.89 ├Ч 107Nm is found.
A three point bending test is performed to see the inямВuence of shear deformations. The setup is
similar to Figure L.1. The beam is 500mm wide and the distance between supports is 5m. An
area of 250 ├Ч 500mm at mid-span is loaded by a surface load of p = 106N/m2. The deformed
beams are seen in Figure 7.9. Huge shear deformation appear in the core, similar to what is
seen in Appendix L. The purpose of the stiямАness requirement is to limit the deямВections. It is
therefore important to identify the proportion of shear deformations, as the shear deformations
in an orthotropic plate are limited compared to an SPS plate.
The deямВection from bending can be estimated by an analytical solution for a beam. The surface
load at mid-span is assumed to be a point load: P = 0.25m ┬╖ 0.5m ┬╖ 106NmтИТ2 = 1.25 ├Ч 105N.
The beam bending stiямАness is calculated from the plate bending stiямАness as: EI = 0.5m ┬╖ Dt =
0.5m ┬╖ 9.89 ├Ч 107Nm = 4.95 ├Ч 107Nm2. The maximum deямВection of a beam due to bending
only can be found by Eq. (28) and (29) for simple and clamped supports, respectively, following
Baandrup and Bj├жrre [65].

7.6
New Core Material
The basic idea of the sandwich plate system is to increase the stiямАness of a plate without
increasing the weight. Through numerical testing on the system with various conямБgurations it
has been found that the bending stiямАness can be increased but at the expense of huge shear
deformations. Due to the low shear stiямАness of the core material it is diямГcult to design a proper
section, as large shear deformations result in an undesired deformation behavior. It is therefore
of interest to study the possibility of an enhanced core material.
The density of the material needs to remain low, about 1000kg/m3 or a speciямБc gravity of 1,
to be competitive. A YoungтАЩs modulus of approximately 10GPa is assessed to be adequate to
ensure proper shear deformation. It has been decided not to ямБnd a speciямБc material with these
properties, but instead to justify that such a material could be found. Naturally, money is an
issue when choosing material, but as this is a initial structural study of alternative designs, the
price of the material is of no concern.
Huge developments have been seen for composite materials over the past decade. Composites can
easily be changed and modiямБed to serve a speciямБc purpose. Glass-reinforced plastic (ямБberglass) is
an example thereof which has gained widespread acceptance in many industries such as aircraft,
boats, automobiles, windmills and lately power pylons [66, 67]. The speciямБc gravity of ямБberglass
is 1.5-2, thus, a bit more than desired. A cheaper and lighter alternative to ямБberglass is natural
ямБber reinforced polypropylene composites, where natural ямБbers such as wood ямБber, bamboo ямБber,
wheat straw and kenaf are used. The demands for natural ямБber is excepted to grow up to 50%
annually in the coming years [68]. One of the most promising natural ямБbers is kenaf which is
regarded as an industrial crop in Malaysia. Reportedly composites with kenaf have a speciямБc
gravity as low as 0.75 while the YoungтАЩs modulus is 11-14.5GPa [69, 70].
Another processed material which have shown potential for sandwich elements is aluminum foams
[71, 72]. The weight of the material is low as a large volume fraction is gas-ямБlled pores. The
pores can form an interconnected network (open-cell foams), or they can be sealed (closed-cell
foam) as seen in Figure 7.11. The properties of the foams depends on the porosity. Closed-cell
aluminum foam with 70% porosity is reported to have a YoungтАЩs modulus of approximately
10GPa, the speciямБc gravity is 0.8 [73, 74]. The YoungтАЩs modulus of an open-cell foam with
similar porosity is about 5MPa [75].
Based on a small literature study it is assessed to be realistic to ямБnd a core material with
an elastic modulus of 10GPa and a speciямБc gravity of 1.0. It is therefore chosen to use these
properties for the core material and left to further work to ямБnd a proper material fulямБlling this.
The PoissonтАЩs ratio is set to 0.3.
==PAGE:99==
7.7
Improved Orthotropic Sandwich Plate
85
One of the advantages of the polyurethane, used in SPS, is the strong bonding to the face plates.
If e.g. aluminum foam was used, a new bonding material would be needed. Much research have
been done in this ямБeld and a solution does not seem far away. However, this is not within the
scope of this thesis and therefore left to further work. Perfect bonding between the core and the
face plates is assumed.

It is noted that all hot-spot stresses have increased a bit due to a stiямАer section but still below
the cut-oямАlimit.
For all conямБgurations the deferential deямВections are smaller than for the S.14 plate. It is seen that

thus, there is a potential for reducing the weight. By cutting oямАweight equivalent to 2mm steel
from the top deck plate, the weight of the longitudinal steel is reduced by approximately 4%.
The hot-spot stresses and the deямВections are not close to the limits, thus, further optimization
could be made.

Alternative Solution
7.8
Redesigned Deck System
So far, the investigations of an alternative solution have been somewhat limited by the original
design. Using SPS in the orthotropic plates was investigated and considerations were made on
how to replace the orthotropic plates with SPS. The studies have revealed some of the properties
of sandwich plates. Shear deformations were found to be essential and an improved core material
seems to be a necessity to replace the orthotropic plates.
The original design is based on the properties of orthotropic plates, thus, in order to implement
sandwich plates, the system needs to be rethought. One of the advantages of sandwich plates
is that they span in two directions, limiting the need of traditional diaphragms supporting the
plate across the width. Instead, smaller spans and point-supports can be of interest. This points
in the direction of a space truss. By using a space truss a much more uniform load pattern can
be achieved. Also, a lot of complex welding in the diaphragms would be avoided.
A new concept, using sandwich plates with an internal space truss, have been investigated in an
iterative process. The task is to assess whether the concept is realistic. Only initial investigations
are made as time is limited, and solely the ямБnal concept and results are presented.
It is chosen to keep the outer box design with a 2┬░ slope of the upper plates and one horizontal
and two inclined bottom plates. The investigation is limited to the center part of the box as the
new system transfers the load to the hangers at a diямАerent location. The center part is marked
with blue in Figure 7.13. The contribution of the outer part, marked with red in Figure 7.13,
is not taken into account. It is chosen to support the plates every 3m in both direction. This
results in a deck segment length of 24m instead of 25m which was used in the original design.
The width of the box under consideration is 24m.
The top and the bottom plates are sandwich plates. The improved core material presented in
section 7.6 is used. First, the top plate is considered. The minimum stiямАness of the top plate
can be estimated from Eurocode guidelines for orthotropic decks.
Figure C.4 in EN-1993-2
[42] provides a minimum stiямАness of a stringer. With 3m distance between cross girders, the
minimum stiямАness can be extrapolated to 2000cm3 for a stringer under the most heavily loaded
traямГc lane. The width of a stringer is typically 600mm. Using steel, a minimum plate bending
stiямАness can be estimated to Dmin = 2000cm4/600mm ┬╖ 210GPa = 7 ├Ч 106Nm.
The sandwich plate conямБguration is estimated by Eq. (24). In the original design, the equivalent
thickness of the top plate was 24-26mm. To save weight, the face plates are set to 10mm. The
minimum core thickness is found to be 66mm. 70mm is used. An equivalent plate stiямАness
Dt = 7.70 ├Ч 106Nm is found, 10% more than the minimum stiямАness.
The bending stiямАness of the bottom plate in the original design is approximately one third of the
top plate. Using this ratio and Eq. (24), the bottom plate is conямБgured with 6mm face plates
and a 50mm core. The equivalent plate stiямАness is Dt = 2.29 ├Ч 106Nm.

Top sandwich plate as a beam
The top sandwich plate is tested as a beam to see the inямВuence of shear deformations. The
test is identical to the tests presented in Appendix L. The setup, supports and load application
are explained in the Appendix. A continuum shell model, which includes shear deformations,
and a shell model using STRI3 elements ([28]), which neglects shear deformations, are tested.
The deformed beams with simple and clamped supports are shown in Figure 7.14 and 7.15,
respectively.

7.8.2
Response of the top sandwich plate
The response of the top sandwich plate to vehicle 5A-H is tested. The top plate is supported
by the space truss every 3m in both directions. A connection piece is intended to ensure zero
rotational restraint of the truss members and to support the top plate through a 500mm ├Ч
500mm support plate. The support plate ensures a better load spread and controls the curvature
of the top face plate. The thickness of the support plates is 20mm.
A test top sandwich plate with support plates, extending a couple of sections, is made as seen
in Figure 7.16a. The model is made with continuum shells. Three elements are used in the
thickness of the face plates, while four elements are used for the core.
The element size is
increased from 0.2m at the outer edge to 0.01m at the location of interest. The vertical support
at the support plates is introduced trough a reference point. The reference point is connected
to the edge of a circle with a diameter of 0.2m as seen in Figure 7.16b. The circle is thereby
considered as a cross-section, which can rotate around the reference point. The supporting is
made to reямВect the situation with an underlying truss in the best way possible without modeling
the truss. At this stage of the study, the supports are accepted, however, hard supports would
not be adequate for a ямБnal investigation.
Vehicle 5A-H is placed at diямАerent locations of the top plate to ямБnd worst case situations. The
parameters set for assessment are the maximum diямАerential deямВections, to ensure comfort when
crossing the bridge, and the maximum curvature, to ensure zero cracking of the surfacing. The
maximum curvature is compared to the maximum curvature experienced by the orthotropic deck.
In Figure 7.6a, the maximum curvature over a rib wall is found to ╬║max = тИТ0.023radтИТ1. The
maximum deямВection is assessed by a comparison to the maximum deямВection between diaphragms
in the original design. In Appendix N.1, the maximum diямАerential deямВection is found to umax =
1.2mm when axle C-E is located between diaphragms.
The inямВuence on the response between axle A, B and C-E is limited as the distance between
supports is only 3m. Thus, the response to axle A, B and C-E are considered independently. As in
previous investigations, axle A is not close to give maximum results, thus, not considered further.
Axle B and C-E is placed at three diямАerent locations each, to provide maximum deямВections and
curvatures. The results in terms of deямВections and curvatures for all six load cases are presented
in Appendix O. In the following, only the worst cases are considered.
The maximum displacement is found when axle C-E is placed in between four supports as shown
in Figure 7.17a. Red marks the wheel sets, while yellow indicates where the nearest support
plates are located underneath the plate. The deямВections in the top face plate are shown in
Figure 7.17b. The maximum deямВection is found to 1.27mm, thus, a bit more than what was
found between diaphragms in the original design.

7.8.3
Deck section with a space truss
The top and bottom sandwich plate are connected by a truss system. How to make the optimal
truss system is huge task, and truly, topology optimization would be a powerful tool. As time
is limited, a simple system is investigated and optimized through a couple of iterations.
The truss system is designed to perform identical to the original design in a тАЩtraямГc jamтАЩ situation.
The traямГc jam consists solely of vehicle 5A-H spaced with 15m in between each front axle in
all three traямГc lanes in both direction. The maximum deямВection in the top plate is then taken
as the deямВection requirement of the new design in a similar situation. The original designed is
tested with the level 2 model, described in section 4, with a setup identical to the one presented
in the following. The result is shown in Appendix N.2. The deямВection requirement of the top
plate is umax = 8.9mm.
All connections in the truss are made with hinges, thus, no bending will appear in the truss
members. The buckling capacity of each member is of no interest at this point of the design
process, whereby only the axial stiямАness is of interest. In a ямБnal design, pipe-proямБles would
be used to ensure a proper bending stiямАness but for simpliямБcation, ямБlled circular proямБles are
used.
A limitation is that the top plate should be supported in a 3m by 3m grid as decided in the
preceding. Initially, solely longitudinal trusses were introduced, whereby the transverse system
would be a vierendeel-system. However, it was quickly found that a transverse truss system was
needed. In particular, a transverse truss is needed at hangers but also in between.
The current design is divided into a longitudinal system and a transverse system as seen in
Figure 7.19. The longitudinal system consists of 9 longitudinal V-trusses. The members are
divided into 5 groups with diямАerent proямБles. The 5 groups are shown in Figure 7.20a. The
transverse system consists of 2 two-legged trusses, one at the hangers and one in between. As
for the longitudinal system, 5 groups are used as shown in Figure 7.20b. Hereby, a total of 10
cross-sections were adjusted in the simple iterative optimization of the truss. The cross-sectional
parameters of each group are shown in Appendix P. Naturally, the biggest members are located
close to the hangers. As will be discussed, huge transverse members connecting to the hangers
were needed due to large shearing.
Five deck segments are connected to form the test model. Vertical supports are provided at each
location of a hanger. The deck is loaded by 48 Vehicle 5A-H, simulating a traямГc jam situation.
The test set-up is shown in Figure 7.21. Red marks the wheel sets of vehicle 5A-H.
The deformations in three most center deck segments are shown in Figure 7.22. The maximum
deямВection is 8.7mm, 0.2mm less than what was found for the original design.

7.8.4
Deck weight
Beside improved manufacturing procedures, the weight is of great importance in the assessment
of the system. A comparison is made between the original and the new design. For the original
design only the center part of the plates and diaphragm, marked with blue in Figure 7.13, and
the internal rods are considered. The weight of three categories, plates, diaphragms and internal
rods, and the total weight are calculated in Appendix Q. The weight of the new design, is simply
calculated by Abaqus. The weight per meter of the deck of each part and the share of plates and
longitudinal and transverse steel are shown in Table 7.6. The total weight of the new design is
20% higher than the original. If the weight of the surfacing is included, the weight is increased
by 12%.
The connection pieces are not included in the above. At the moment the thickness of the support
plates is 20mm. As an estimate, the whole connection piece is considered equivalent to a 30mm
plate. Hereby, the total weight of the new design would be increased by 3%.

Alternative Solution
7.8.5
Discussion
A new structural design of the deck has been proposed. Through simple deямВection considerations,
the design was made to perform as good as the original design. Simple iterative optimization
was performed to reduce the weight of the truss system. However, the the ямБnal weight was found
to be 20% higher than the weight of the original design. Many assumptions had to be made
to simplify the design process, thus, uncertainties are involved and no clear conclusion will be
made. Instead, the design and optimization possibilities are discussed, and the potential of the
system is assessed.
A potential advantage of the new design is the connection details. Using orthotropic plates
involves a lot of welding and includes many critical details.
In the introduction (section 1)
it was found that fabrication of the orthotropic panels was covered by 17% of the total deck
cost of the Great Belt Bridge. Today, for a long span bridge such as the Great Belt Bridge,
the orthotropic panel fabrication cost was estimated to 191 million DKK while the assembling
to deck segments cost was estimated to 302 million DKK (Appendix A). The new system is
designed so that only one standardized connection piece is used. This piece could probably be
a one piece cast, minimizing the need of welding. By standardizing the connection, thousands
identical pieces can be made at a lower cost. At this point, it is obviously diямГcult to say anything
about how much more simple such connections would be, but as welding in orthotropic steel
decks is a huge expense, the potential for a cost reduction is big.
The original design have been used and developed for 60 years, and there is not much potential
left for optimizing the system further. This became clear in the background study in section 2,
where most optimization studies where about changing plate thicknesses a couple of millimeters.
In the new design, a huge potential is seen for optimizing the system to meet diямАerent demands.
Changing member sizes and ямБnding the optimal spacing is something which cannot be solved
manually, thus, optimization algorithms should be used. As described in section 7.2, a large
untapped potential is seen in topology optimization which would be ideal for this kind of task.
The idea of having a longitudinal system and a transverse system is based on the original
concept and was used due to simple implementation.
However, a much more optimal truss
system, which do not diямАerentiate between longitudinal and transverse system, can be found.
Also, an investigation should be made on how to improve the angles in the truss. For now,
the angle is simply a result of the original deck height and the spacing of three meters. The
maximum angle between the plates and the truss is 72┬░.
From the weight shares shown in Table 7.6, the potential of optimizing each category can be
assessed. The weight proportion of the plates is far the biggest. The sandwich plates used in the
new design are heavier than the orthotropic plates in the original design. Although the density
of the core material is set to nearly one eighth of steel, the sandwich plates are heavier due to
a thick core. Examples of materials with proper elastic properties and lower speciямБc gravities
than the applied (1.0) were found in section 7.6. It is therefore of interest to see how low the
density of the core material needs to be in order to obtain the same weight as in the original
design. From the diямАerence in weight of 1789kg/m a speciямБc gravity of 0.383 can be found for
the core material. Thus, the density of the core should be reduced by two thirds to maintain
the weight from the original design. Whether this is possible is left for further study, but it is
noticed that due to the huge share in weight of the plates, a reduction in core density have a
huge impact.
The core is needed to ensure proper bending stiямАness of the plate. For the top plate, bending
stiямАness is needed to limit deямВections, however, this is not the case for the bottom plate. The
==PAGE:109==
7.8
Redesigned Deck System
95
deck acts as a continuous beam, whereby negative moments will occur at the locations of hangers.
Hereby, the bottom deck plate is in compression and a bending stiямАness is needed to prevent
buckling. In between hangers, the deck moment will be positive, and the bottom plate will be
without buckling risk whereby less bending stiямАness is needed. To utilize this, the core thickness
of the bottom plate could vary along the deck so that less core material is used. Obviously, a
lot of research is needed to take advantage of this, but with the sandwich plate it is possible
opposed to limitations set by using orthotropic plates.
Another way to reduce the core thickness is by decreasing the span length between support
plates, as lower bending stiямАnesses of the sandwich plats would be required.
Instead, more
material would be used on the truss system. Assessing which is the most weight eямГcient is
a huge investigation, but deямБnitely this is something which should be considered. In case the
span is reduced, the V-trusses can possibly be replaced by X-trusses in order to obtain optimal
angles.
The new design neglects the outer part of the deck section marked with blue in Figure 7.13.
Naturally, this part adds stiямАness to the system, thus, if added, the new design would be expected
to perform even better. In the ends of the diaphragms in the original design, a thick steel plate
is embedded to deal with the huge shear force near the hanger. Details such as this adds a lot
of weight to the original design, which was not included in the weight calculation. Therefore, in
order better to make an assessment, a full deck section with the new concept should be designed
so that a more appropriate weight comparison can be made.
The redesign of the deck involves a lot of uncertainty and many simpliямБed considerations. A
truss system with sandwich plates was made and tested to perform adequately. Although the
new system was found to increase the weight of steel by 20%, the system has a huge potential.
The system can be optimized in many ways, and the amount of diямГcult details are radically
reduced. Hereby not said that the connection piece described is without complications, but
as it is the same detail all over, the procedures can be optimized and developed with greater
beneямБts.
==PAGE:110==
==PAGE:111==
8
Conclusion
Based on a detailed and extensive ямБnite element model, the current design and alternative
solutions of the deck design for the Izmit Bay Bridge were investigated.
Shell elements with orthotropic behavior and beam elements with proper interaction properties
were developed to make an FE-model which take into account the overall stiямАness of the bridge.
Additionally, simpliямБed analytical solutions were used to limit the time used on the modeling.
Acceptable performance of the individual parts and the interaction between members was found.
The inямВuence of the many assumptions on the ямБnal results was found acceptable through an
extra detailed model.
Fatigue evaluations methods were discussed. Although in practice, the nominal stress approach
is used, the hot-spot stress approach was found the most suitable for the investigation. Critical
details were identiямБed with an initial lane centered vehicle position. The rib-to-deck connection
and the diaphragm cut-out were studied.
Through BettiтАЩs theorem, inямВuence surfaces of the two details were made, and a worst case wheel
load location was identiямБed. The inямВuence at rib-to-deck connection was found very localized,
thus, the detail was solely impacted by a single set of wheels. Meanwhile, the inямВuence at the
cut-out extended to the adjacent diaphragms and over the width of the bridge, whereby several
axles had impact. Due to the localized inямВuence at the rib-to-deck connection, the wheel load
location has a huge say. The hot-spot stresses increased approx. 30%, by locating the wheel load
directly above the detail of interest. The maximum stress in the cut-out increased by 3%.
Model considerations were made on how detailed and extensive to make the model. Too simpli-
ямБed boundary conditions showed a huge stress increase at the cut-out and questionable results
at the rib-to-deck connection, although a very localized rib-to-deck inямВuence was found at ямБrst.
A model similar to the one used in the COWI design veriямБcation, neglecting three quarters of
the deck section and with presumed supports, was found to be too ямВexible resulting in reduced
stresses. Most signiямБcant at the cut-out, with a 35% reduction, but also, more unexpectedly,
huge reductions were found at the rib-to-deck connections. Taking into account the overall stiямА-
ness, in terms of hangers, cables and pylons, was found to have a big inямВuence on the otherwise
very local fatigue stresses. Using a non-linear calculation with dead load and pre-tensioned ca-
bles and hangers was found to be of less importance, although small stress reductions were seen
at the cut-outs.
Several options on how to improve the design were discussed. The sandwich plate system with an
elastomer core was studied in an orthotropic design and in an attempt to replace the orthotropic
plates. Due to huge shear deformations, simply replacing the orthotropic plates with SPS was
rejected. Replacing the top deck plate with an SPS plate was found to be possible, but due to
a large shear to bending deformation ratio, the design is still questionable.
==PAGE:112==
98
8
Conclusion
An improved core material was suggested and reasoned.
For the orthotropic design with a
sandwich top deck plate, naturally, better deямВection behavior was seen. Compared to the original
design, the increased plate bending stiямАness resulted in signiямБcantly reduced hot-spot stresses.
A potential for reducing the weight of the design was found, but due to increased fabrication
costs, no ямБnal conclusion can be derived.
Finally, a new deck design concept with sandwich plates and an internal space truss was sug-
gested. The improved core material in the sandwich plates ensured reasonable shear deforma-
tions. The deformation response from wheel loads was studied and compared to the original
design. A тАЩsmoothтАЩ deformation behavior was seen with small curvatures ensuring good perfor-
mance of the surfacing. However, bigger diямАerential deямВections were found, and further studies
and requirements are needed to give ямБnal conclusions. An internal truss system with a longi-
tudinal and a transverse system was suggested. Simple iterative optimization was performed
to reduce the truss members. The ямБnal design was estimated to increase the weight by 20%.
However, huge potential for optimization was found and many simpliямБcations was argued to be
favorable for a ямБnal design of the new concept.
==PAGE:113==
9
Further Work
The most optimal design of the deck in a long span bridge is probably unachievable. Nevertheless,
the research already done for orthotropic decks seems to come very close. Only small diямАerences
are made in the design following new challenges found in existing bridges. In order to achieve
a greater reduction in fabrication costs, new solutions and probably an entirely new concept is
needed. Such were investigated and assessed in the report, but a lot of research is still needed
before the potential new solutions can be used.
Firstly, an attempt was made to make an extended FE-model of the bridge used for fatigue
evaluation. Many simpliямБcations and assumptions were made, and many more hours could be
used on testing all diямАerent members in order to include the coupling stiямАnesses for all orthotropic
plates. Beam elements were developed to include the shearing in the spikes under interaction
with the orthotropic plates. But do to limited time, very rough simpliямБcations had to be made.
Taking into account the overall stiямАness was found to have big inямВuence, and it is therefore of
interest to do more research on how to simplify the model and use transitions between diямАerent
levels of detailing in order to keep the computational cost as low as possible. The shell elements
developed with orthotropic behavior are rather unorthodox.
Further study should be made
on whether they can be used in an actual model and how the simpliямБcation otherwise can be
made.
Suggestions for alternative solutions changing the system to lesser and greater extends were
presented. The solution replacing the top deck plate with SPS has a potential, but the deямВection
behavior over rib walls is still questionable and the increased cost is unknown. Further research
is needed to answer these questions.
An initial search for a new core material was made to increase its performance. A material
with an elasticity modulus of 10GPa and a speciямБc gravity of 1.0 was justiямБed. However, much
research is yet to be done to deямБne a such or an even better core material. Also, research should
be made on how to ensure the needed bonding between the face plates and the core.
Solely initial studies were made on the new deck design concept. Thus, much more research can
be done to improve the system. The response of the sandwich plates to diямАerent vehicle types
should be studied and the diямАerential deямВection criteria should be deямБned. The piece connecting
the plates and the truss needs to be developed and optimized to ensure acceptable curvatures
and a low weight as possible. The proper rigidity of the connection should be studied to ямБnd
whether the joints should acts as hinges or rigidly.
A huge potential is seen in the truss system which can be optimized in many ways. Topology
optimization would be a powerful tool for this task. The idea of having a longitudinal and a
transverse system should probably be discarded so that the truss system instead is a space truss
acting optimally in both directions. The interaction with the sandwich plates should be studied
to ямБnd the optimal span length for the plates and optimal angles in the truss. To ensure better
==PAGE:114==
100
9
Further Work
angles, X-trusses may be a good option which should be studied. The hanger connection point
was chosen rather arbitrarily, thus, this as well should be studied. Finally, the weight of the
system should be studied and compared much more detailed. Beside eventual less complicated
details and fabrication procedures, at least a maintained weight is of most important to ensure
better performance of the entire bridge system.

==PAGE:139==
125
E
Deck Dead Load
In the following appendix deadloads are calculated for the Izmit Bay Bridge. The dead loads are
calculated similarly to what was done in Andersen & Hansen (2012), so that the base state will be
correct once the pre-tension forces given in Andersen & Hansen (2012) are applied.
In order to have the same dead load along the bridge the deadload found for the beam level 1 model
is spread out to all the plates of the level 2 and 3 model. It is assumed that it is sufficient to
destribute the dead load to the density of the plates, thus the diaphram beams will not have any
density. In the level 3 model the dead load is distributed to the density of the top plate only thus ribs
and diaphragm beams have no density.
The weight of the surfacing is only distributed to the top plates of the deck. It is noted that the width
of the total bridge deck was used in the Andersen & Hansen (2012) model to calculate the weight of
the surfacing, while it is assumed that the surfacing only exist on the top plates in the present
model. In order to maintian the dead load of the deck section, the surfacing weight from Andersen &
Hansen (2012) is distributed to the top plates of the present model.

An approximation of the shear stiffness is calculated for the deck plate alone, as the ribs won't have much
influence on this property.
Shear stiffness:
D33.TP1

The shell element used to model the plate in Abaqus only has one twist stiffness. As the plate is
orthotropic an assumption has to be made in order to see whether a representive single twist stiffeness
can be calculated analytically, instead of having one for each direction. As a hugh different is found on
the torsional stiffness in the two directions it is assumed that the twist stiffness is can be represented as
an average of the two torsional stiffnesses. As the stiffness parameter used in the stiffness matrix
couples mxy and 2╬║xy the stiffness parameter is found as:
==PAGE:143==

D66.TP1
GKtor.1
GKtor.2
+
2
2.897
106
├Ч
N m2
тЛЕ
m
тЛЕ
=

The last property to be determined is the transverse shear stiffness. Following Nielsen (2008) the weak-axial
transverse shear stiffness can be determined from the deck plate alone, while the strong-axial transverse
shear stiffness is determined based on a closed thinwalled section, whereby only the rib webs contributes.
The coupling transverse shear stiffness is equal to zero (Nielsen (2008)):

Orthotropic Plate Meshing Test
When using ямБnite elements it is important to check whether the results have converged. Exam-
ples of such checks are presented in the following.
Bending
The ямБrst convergence test is a strong-axial bending test of the orthotropic plate as presented in
section 4.2.5. Two diямАerent shell elements has been tested; a 4-node shell element (S4R) and
an 8-node shell element (S8R) both with reduced integration. The mesh is deямБned by the mesh
size. As the ribs are 0.3m wide the largest mesh size investigated is 0.3m. The strong-axial
curvature as a function of mesh-size is seen in Figure G.1.
Mesh size [m]
0
0.05
0.1
0.15
0.2

2.6
4-node element
8-node element
Figure G.1: Mesh convergence of the orthotropic plate under twisting.
It is found that the 8-node shell element performs much better than the 4-node shell element, as
a converged result to a satisfactory degree is seen for all mesh sizes. Meanwhile the 4-node shell
element has not converged by use of the mesh sizes presented. Though, it is seen that the result
of the 4-node shell element converges towards the result of the 8-node shell element. Clearly, the
most eямГcient model is obtained by using the 8-node shell element with a larger mesh size. A
reason for the quick convergence of the 8-node shell element is the smooth deformation as seen
in Figure 4.12a, which is secured by minimal number of boundary conditions and constrains in
the model.
Twisting
The second mesh convergence test is of an orthotropic plate loaded in twisting. Two types of shell
elements are tested (S4R and S8R). As for the bending test the largest mesh size is 0.3m, while
the smallest is 0.01m/0.02m (S4R/S8R) due to computational limitations. The twist curvature
as a function of mesh-size is seen in Figure G.2.
For both types of elements a converging behavior is seen, though not as fast as for the bend-
ing test. The reason for this is the much more complicated type of deformation where local

deformations are found in between the ribs as seen in Figure 4.15. It is noted that the results

it is found that the 8-node shell element performs most eямГciently.

Initial Test Model
In the following appendix the connection between the hangers and the deck is tested as well as
the connection between the level 3 model deck and the level 2 model deck. Two load cases have
been tested in order to ensure correct behavior of the ямБnal model. The test set-up is seen in
Figure I.1.
Beam
Beam
Beam
Beam
(a) Load case 1.
Beam
Beam
Beam
Beam
(b) Load case 2.
Figure I.1: Test set-up.
The hangers are connected to the deck by use of a connector section which is deямБned through a
wire. The connector section is deямБned as a beam constraining all DOFs. A coupling constraint
is used to connect the level 1 model deck to the shell elements of the level 2 model deck as seen
in Figure I.2.
Beam
Beam
Beam
Beam
Figure I.2: Coupling constraints.
The deformations and rotations of the test model is seen in Figure I.3 and I.4 for the two load
cases, respectively.
==PAGE:160==
146
Appendix I
(a) Deformations.
(b) Rotations.
Figure I.3: Results of load case 1.
(a) Deformations.
(b) Rotations.
Figure I.4: Results of load case 2.
From both load cases it is seen that the connector section and the coupling constraints does so
that the rotations and translations are the same in the connected elements. The idea of having
two hangers with an appropriate distance in between seems to be working. The connection
between the beam elements and the shell elements is also veriямБed.

Modeling of SPS
A sandwich beam is investigated in order better to understand the FEA results when using
sandwich plates and to identify diямАerences between diямАerent modeling techniques. Several types
of elements are studied, reference is made to the Abaqus manual [28].
The following test beam is used for the investigation. The thickness of the steel face plates is
10mm and the polyurethane core is 90mm. Room temperature is assumed. The characteristics
of the the polyurethane were presented in Table 7.1. The beam is 500mm wide, and the length
between the supports is 3m. The beam is loaded with a 250├Ч500 mm surface load of magnitude
p = 106N/m2 at midspan. Initially two boundary conditions are investigated; simply supported
and clamped. The setups are shown in Figure L.1.
X
Y
Z
(a) Simply supported.
X
Y
Z
(b) Clamped.
Figure L.1: Test SPS beam setup.
Modeling Technique
Initially, two modeling techniques are investigated. The most comprehensive option is to use
solid elements as each layer is made by several elements. Continuum shells (SC8R) are suitable
for this method. The other option is conventional shells, where the layering is deямБned in the
section deямБnition. S8R with 6 DOFs and 3 integration points in each layer is used for this option.
It is noted that both elements are capable of handling shear deformations.
The deямВection results from the three point bending tests are shown in Figure L.2 and L.3. The
deямВection using shell elements is found to be 5.2% and 8.9% smaller than for the continuum shell
model for simple supports and clamped, respectively. The diямАerence is due to the continuum shell
being better to handle the large shear deformation in the core. The kink at the simple supports
is caused by shear deformations which govern the deformation. The huge shear deformation is
easily identiямБed from the diamond shaped core elements shown in Figure L.4.

Shear deformations
A new model is made to identify the size of the shear deformations compared to the deямВections
caused by bending. Triangular thin shells (STRI3) follows KirchhoямАтАЩs plate theory and neglect
shear deformations. The STRI3 elements are used in the conventional shell model. The deformed
beams with simple and clamped supports are shown in Figure L.5. The maximum deямВections
are compared to results using continuum shells. For the simple supported beam it is found that
the deямВection due to shear is half the deямВection caused by bending. For the clamped beam the
deямВection due to shear is almost twice the deямВection from bending.
Equivalent properties
In the literature ([62, 63, 64]) equivalent properties are suggested to be used with SPS as dis-
cussed in section 7.3.1. It is therefore of interest to compare a model using equivalent properties
with the results from the previous sections. The equivalent plate stiямАness is calculated by use
of Eq. (24). The total thickness is 110mm.

The results of the three point bending tests using equivalent YoungтАЩs modulus and PoissonтАЩs
ratio are shown in Figure L.6 and L.7. The deямВections are close to the result from the thin shell

One way to include the low shear stiямАness in the equivalent model is by prescribing the correct
shear stiямАness when deямБning the section. The shearing is assumed to be limited to the core
whereby the shear stiямАness is calculated from Eq. (27).

The simpliямБed model overestimates the the deямВection by 1.1% and 4.3% for the simply supported
and clamped beam, respectively. The model performs better than the layered shell model but
overestimates the maximum deямВection instead of underestimating it. The equivalent properties
with a reduced shear stiямАness could possibly be an adequate solution if only the stiямАness of the
plate is of interest. However, the section stress distribution would give false results. As the local
stress distribution is of interest in the investigations, the simpliямБed model will not be used.

is only 3m, thus, the inямВuence between axle A, B and C-E is limited. Axle C-E and B are
